RATING: 2.5
Another game that is STRICTLY WORSE as a boardgame as opposed to being a PC game.

+ Yay dice. 

- We secretly roll dice and secretly assign them, behind a screen. What?! This is the greatest invitation to cheating i have ever seen in any game. 
"Don't play with cheaters."
Don't design games to have glaring holes that can be exploited at any moment by anyone without any consequences.

- Zero interaction. Literally. Zero. Interaction. The game has four hundred components and they manage to put ZERO interaction in. Invade others' planets. Work together on some developments. Trade goods. Kill their workforce, fight with ships, bribe, bluff, negotiate... Nope. None of it. The game BEGS to be interactive and they manage to destroy all traces of life in it. Shut up, sit down, stare at your part of the game, collect points, go home. 
Singleplayer multiplayer boardgames are the worst.

- There's no reason to play this live. You gain NOTHING from playing live that you couldn't gain online. That's a mark of a miserable game. It is only more fiddly, clunky, chaotic, slow. 

- A bunch of rules that flavorfully make no sense. The kind you put in to make the game flow exactly as planned, with no regard to flavor. Another classic example of good game design.

2.5/10
Enjoy your spot in the top 100, Roll. You're everything that most BGG raters want.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
It's a good game, and I'm enjoying playing it a lot, but some of the design choices are clunky: It is unintuitive to place dice results onto phase spaces where the symbols don't match (also to do the same thing when re-assigning dice); the hidden turns behind the screens are an odd feature - totally understand why they are there - but in a game with so much unintuitive stuff going on, this opens up a lot of scope for mistakes by new players going un-noticed; the gameplay is totally uninteractive.

That said, I love simultaneous play, and RFTG scores big on that point. The engine-building is very satisfying, the game length is just right, and the game seems well balanced. I like the aesthetics too.

My favourite genre is dice-rolling, and I have many such games, but this game doesn't feel like a dice game in any real sense. It has MANY dice included, but the rolling of the dice is of minimal importance in the overall gameplay (because dice can be placed on non-matching spaces etc.). There are few opportunities to re-roll dice, combine dice, add up results, push-your-luck: all staples of the dice genre. I have chosen to think of this as an engine-building game to sit alongside San Juan and others of that ilk. As a dice game it disappoints.

That all said, overall, it's a pretty nice package.

RATING: 4
I am genuinely amazed. A dice game that is actually harder to teach and more complicated than the original game. And still, Roll for the Galaxy manages to be shallower and a less satisfying experience. 

RollftG does not offer the same agonizing trade-offs as its mother because you are not closing doors when paying for the cards in your hand. The only good thing left in RollftG is the action selection and leeching, which is now twice as complicated as before. Usually you would expect complex games to have a lot of depth, since you are balancing between many things, but not in RollftG. You just have a number of choices with a myriad of restrictions but none of them seemed very interesting.

Due to the unnecessary and non-rewarding complexity and the lack of trade-offs, I most likely won't play this again.

RATING: 8.5
Fantastic game!  I wouldn't go so far as to call it a Race for the Galaxy killer, but my gaming group likes Roll better than Race because of Race's difficult iconography.

Roll is fast and fun with lots of strategy.  I prefer games like Roll with input randomness (i.e. the random element happens and then you make decisions on that out, rather than having a random element determine the outcome of your decisions), and the random factor in Roll makes for challenging decision making while also being something you can affect by choosing which dice you add to your civilization.

RATING: 8
+ Tactical Decisions
You are constantly figuring out how to use your resources as efficiently as possible. It may feel like you are at the mercy of luck when you are rolling dice or drawing tiles, but there is almost always a way to turn things in your favor. Every round is a challenging puzzle that is rewarding to solve.  

+ Reading Other Players
An important part of the game is being able to anticipate what other players will do so that you can take advantage of their phase selection. It is especially gratifying when you are able to pull this off. 

+ Satisfying Engine Building
Building your empire by gaining dice from settlements and acquiring special abilities from developments is fun. There is always something to look forward to.

- Multiplayer Solitaire
Because most of the game takes place behind the player screens, it is easy to focus on your own tableau and ignore the rest of the table. Even if you are paying attention, it is hard to keep track of what everyone is doing and what their special abilities are. This is probably something that will get better with more plays and familiarity with the game.

RATING: 7.5
Being a big fan of tableau building games, I quite enjoy Roll for the Galaxy. I've been looking for a cool heavier dice game for quite some time and so far this is the best one I've played!

As far as visual impact is concerned, while I really like the artwork in this game, I wish that the graphic design could be a little bit clearer and less gray. As it is, it's functional but not quite so eye-catchy and still too busy at times. I have a mixed impression of the component quality as well. On the one hand I absolutely love the dice! They're good quality, have bright colors and a well thought-out iconography. I also like the thickness of the tile, but - on the other hand - I unfortunately cannot say the same about the player boards, which are too thin for my taste. 

Mechanism-wise instead build combos and cool interactions is one of my favorite things in games and Roll for the Galaxy offers this in spades! There's just so many ways to personalize your game, as you develop new technology and settle new planets, and there's also an excellent replay value in the base box already. Yet I also find myself enjoying a lot the element of dice manipulation that this title offers. Ever since the Voyages of Marco Polo I've been very intrigued by this kind of mechanism and Roll for the Galaxy makes no exception. 

Unfortunately though I do have a few minor dislike about the game. For one thing there's not really much interaction between the players besides enabling the actions and therefore the whole game can feel really solitaire, especially with 4 or 5 players. Also there's a considerable amount of luck, in particular concerning the drawing of the tiles. Depending on your strategy and the various synergies that you have built in front of you, you might need to draw a certain type of tile and yet lose a great deal of explore actions trying to find something useful. There're ways around this problem, but it's still hard to control, ironically much more so than the dice.

For these reasons my enjoyment of the game isn't as high as it could have been. Still I quite enjoy Roll for the Galaxy, especially at lower player counts. A solid game that I'll play gladly from time to time.

RATING: 8
1st play: Wow, when I started hearing this game was good, I thought there was no way it could live up to the hype. After enjoying the Alien Orb expansion cards, and hating the orb, I was weary of this new twist to one of my old favorites. This game delivers, and might actually be a slight improvement over the original at higher player counts. I can't remember the last time my game-group buddies and I played a new game twice in one night(Aside from 15 minute social deduction games), but this one we did.

2nd play: Still excellent, the decisions to make each round are fun, the dice push you in one way or another, but you can still control your fate. I'm looking forward to the inevitable expansion at some point, but the game doesn't need it. This is an improvement over race in some ways, but not so much that it replaces it. Solid entry in the "for the galaxy" trilogy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm not a fan of dice rolling. When a well executed plan fails because of a dice roll, it just frustrates me - even if it happens to someone else resulting in my favor. However, in RollFTG, your dice rolls can largely be mitigated in such a way that luck is greatly reduced and can usually still be saved to execute your plan or even lead you down another favorable path.

Being able to bet on how the other player(s) will execute a phase is fun... Even when the plan doesn't work out, it's great to see how everyone was hoping the dice would play out. I also enjoy the multiple avenues to victory, being able to watch what your opponents are doing to gauge out how well off you are and to be able to adjust your plan to get a leg up and get back in the running again. I've been able to adjust my plan every time to accommodate what's on the board and pull out big score runs quickly. Very satisfying! Locked in at 45 minutes regardless of player count is a little bit of a let down. I wish it were just slightly longer, but it doesn't deter me overall. Better to end too soon than to drag too long.

This is a beast for me to wrap my head around and teach. Since everything is pretty much executed at the same time, I find it difficult to explain this game in a logical format. Once the rules are understood though, it's easy to play.

We haven't played this is a while, but I'd always be in for a game.

RATING: 6
Race for the Galaxy is a favourite game and inevitably that colours my reaction to Roll...

Roll is a clever implementation of the action selection and tableau building of Race using dice and tiles.  On brief acquaintance, the luck of the dice seems about the same as the luck of the cards; the dice have plenty of wild results and several developments offer the power to re-arrange them. Nonetheless, I prefer the speed and simplicity of choosing an action card over rolling, arranging and re-arranging a bunch of dice.  Ultimately, I don't think that Roll improves on Race, nor does it offer me a significantly different experience.  However, gamers without my baggage may find Roll the more accessible game. 

Production quality is very good.  The dice needed to be small, but the icons on them are clear.  The artwork is good, though less prominent than in Race.

Το εξαιρετικό Race for the Galaxy σε έκδοση με ζάρια και tiles αντί για κάρτες?? Μμμμ όχι ακριβώς. Το Rollftg πατάει πάνω στους δοκιμασμένος μηχανισμούς του Rtfg αλλά είναι ξεχωριστό παιχνίδι και έχει δικιά του αίσθηση και προσωπικότητα. 

Μια σημαντική αλλαγή είναι η προσθήκη του χρήματος σαν resource στο παιχνίδι. Τα χρήματα καθορίζουν πόσα ζάρια έχεις στην διάθεση σου κάθε γύρα ενώ στο Race οι κάρτες είναι ταυτόχρονα και χρήμα. Αυτό πρακτικά σημαίνει πως ο παίκτης πλέον δεν κοιτάει ένα πλήθος καρτών με διάφορα abilities και δεν χρειάζεται να διαλέγει συνεχώς τι να κρατήσει και τι να πετάξει. Άρα το παιχνίδι είναι σαφώς πιο φιλικό προς τους νέους παίκτες καθώς γλυτώνουν την μεγάλη και συνεχή ροή πληροφοριών που τους δυσκολεύει στο Race. 

Μια άλλη αλλαγή είναι πως πλέον πρώτα γίνεται η παραγωγή των υλικών και μετά η κατανάλωση. Αυτή είναι μια πιο φυσική αλληλουχία των πραγμάτων και πλέον μπορείς να βρεις ένα πλανήτη μέσω exploration, να τον αποικήσεις με settle, να παράγεις και να πουλήσεις την παραγωγή στην ίδια γύρα. 

Για όποιον φοβάται τα ζάρια να πω πως η τύχη παίζει μικρό ρόλο, δεν υπάρχουν κακιές ζαριές. Το παιχνίδι προσφέρει μεγάλη παραμετροποίηση των ζαριών. Απλά να σημειώσω πως δε μου αρέσει που ρίχνουμε τα ζάρια πίσω από την οθόνη μας. Θέλω να υπάρχει διαύγεια.  

Είναι ένα εξαιρετικό παιχνίδι με πολλές επιλογές και αρκετά προσβάσιμο σε νέους παίκτες.

RATING: 7
Initial thoughts after just a few plays: The dice action selection works really well: it's a whole extra layer of tactical decision making every turn. It seems even more important than in Race to guess what other players will do, and it's easy to see from the dice on their build queues and production worlds what they might be up to. On the other hand, it is harder to see what other players' special powers are across the table because they are described in writing. That brings its own big advantage, though, in that there is less of a steep learning curve for newbies as there isn't the same (wonderful) iconography to learn. One generally sees far fewer tiles in this compared to the number of cards you see in Race: I wonder if that means it's harder to find synergies, or whether they are more dependent on the luck of the draw. Just a tentative thought, and I will enjoy finding out as I hope to play this game a lot.

RATING: 10
Includes:
[thing=175754][/thing]

:arrowE: [u]Synopsis[/u]:
I stated in the Puerto Rico entry above that consequent Role Selection games are just not as good. I'd say that's true, with one exception--Roll for the Galaxy.* 

Rather than select a single role on a turn, each player rolls their available dice to represent different phases which will be activated during the round. Multiple phases can be active at the same time, and any reserve dice not allocated to choosing your phase, but which match phases activated by other players, are also consumed and allow for multiple actions each round. Explore tiles, Develop technologies, Settle planets, Produce and Ship goods, and assign dice creatively to manage your economy. 

:arrowE: [u]What I like most[/u]:
Assigning dice! After each player rolls their dice and sees which symbols are available, one (of whatever symbol) is used to select a phase and the rest are placed under their respective phases as potential bonuses. However, every player can set aside a single die to reassign another single die to any phase. On top of that, a number of developments specialize in adding and boosting reassignment power, leading to a TON of "luck" mitigation and very strong control over your economy. Also, those dice are just awesome, and there are so many of them! So many!!!

:arrowE: [u]What I like least[/u]:
While there is a lot of agency available to swap out potential tiles through the Explore phase, there are some games where your available worlds and developments simply don't synergize. You'll still play well, but you're to have to be very crafty to adapt and win by other means.

*[size=7]No, I have not played much Race for the Galaxy, but frankly, I don't care. I so thoroughly enjoy Roll for the Galaxy that I see no reason to attempt to dethrone it. [/size]

:arrowE: [size=13][url=https://play.spotify.com/user/jwilliamparkin/playlist/0GhupDkoKNkOwzSFVtVE2J][b]PLAYLIST[/b][/url][/size]

RATING: 7.25
A game I am only just beginning to grok and properly enjoy. Suffers from an odd play time: 15 minutes too long to be a filler and 15 minutes too short to feel satisfying on its own. For a game so simply about optimal engine building it also feels bizarrely mechanically dilute to me; the dice are easily made inconsequential, some tiles are OP compared to others, and the guessing your opponent's move introduces risk rather than meaningful interaction, in my opinion. I like to feel the engine in a game throb: the heft of each die in Castles of Burgundy does this for me, whereas Roll is a much looser game with little direct system feedback.

RATING: 6
In a few words: Would MUCH rather play Race for the Galaxy

I knew coming in that this really wasn't going to be a game for me because of all of the dice rolling and I was proved correct.

Issues:
You want to do an action and you HOPE that the dice roll in your favor.  Sure, you can mitigate it with some "reassign" cards abilities, but there were times where just had no good options to take.  

Dice games in most instances are easier than their board game counter parts.  Knowing all of the rules of Race for the Galaxy can take a while to learn, but once you know the symbology, you know 99% of the cards.  There are a few exceptions (I'm look at you gambling world!) but in whole, they are explainable just with icons on the card.  In Roll, there are a ton of cards text about reassigning and the reassigning phase just takes too long.

RATING: 10
I've only played a couple of two-player games so far, but I'm really digging this game. Setup only takes a couple of minutes if you sorted the pieces right in your last tear-down, and everything is so tactile. The tiles for planets/developments feel great, the dice are fun to roll, the plastic dice cups are solid and not flimsy, and you're pretty much always doing something. 

There were a couple of rounds where one of us finished quickly and the other was still placing their dice, so there might be some downtime between quick players waiting.  Some see this as a downside, but I see it as time to either talk to other players or read up on the abilities your planets or developments give you.

My first two games lasted 40 and 20 minutes, with the latter a "rush to end conditions" from one player that ended up being a lot of fun.

My only gripe is the rulebook. As someone who's never played Race for the Galaxy, it took a couple of YouTube tutorials to figure out how to actually play this game. The gameplay itself is pretty simple, but the rulebook was a confusing mess on my first read-through. I'm going to try to find a cheat sheet to help me explain the rules to new players.

First impression: This game is on my list of favorites. I'll update my review after a few more playthroughs if anything changes, but, so far, I'd highly recommend it.

RATING: 6
Roll for the Galaxy has some of the best use of dice I have seen in a tabletop game.

This lack of balance is my biggest problem with the game and for me, this is the reason it does not hold a candle to Race for the Galaxy.

RATING: 9
An interesting role-selection, economic snowballing game built of the model of Race of the Galaxy and San Juan. More and more, I've wanted the additional strategies of Race combined with the ease of play of San Juan, and Roll does go in that direction. The developments and worlds in Roll are more like building in San Juan, in having only one ability or granting dice to the player. That ease the requirement for iconography and makes learning easier. The role selection in this game is harsh than in Race: in RftG, it easy to get work around phases that you hoped would be selected but weren't, whereas in Roll, there's no chance to readjust.

My only complaint is that it seems to go by a bit too quick -- it maybe to easy to tableau rush, given you have to build nine worlds/developments instead of 11.

RATING: 7
Nekem jobban tetszik, mint az rftg. Persze ott elegáns az, hogy minimális kellékkel operál (gyakorlatilag csak kártyák és GYP-jelzők vannak), de nekem ez valahogy több, és nem csak a színesebb kellékek miatt. Itt az akcióválasztás jobban ki van találva, és általánosságban a munkások körforgása is jól működik. Könnyebb mint a nagy elődje, és bár nem rövidebb, azért a kockák pakolgatása ellenére is lement fél óra alatt egy kétszemélyes parti. Szerintem 5-en is tuti lemegy egy órán belül a szimultán végrehajtás miatt. Felmerült a fórumokon, hogy a paraván mögött lehet csalni, de végül is ha valaki hajlamos, minden játékban tud csalni. Szerintem ezen a szinten ez egyszerűen nem jellemző. A kockák egyébként sajnos kisebbek mint amit reméltem, így vigyázni kell rá, hogy ne forduljanak el a tologatás közben.
Elég kiegyensúlyozottnak tűnt két fővel, átlagosan 3-4 pont különbséggel végeztünk. Szinte mindig más módon nyertünk.
A nyelvfüggőség ellenére is sokkal letisztultabbnak éreztem a lapkákat, mint az Rftg többfunkciós lapjait. Itt sokkal kevesebb fajta ikon van egyébként. A garantált 1 pénz jó dolog, de azért így is meg lehet akadni, ha nem gondolkozunk előre.
Az alapkoncepció nagyon hasonló maradt (fázisok, bolygó színek, stb.), mégis sokkal másabb lett mint az előd. Szerintem jól meg fognak férni egymás mellett:) Én szívesebben játszom a kockással, de ma már az Rftg-nek sem vagyok úgy ellene, mint amikor először kipróbáltam.
Összességében megértem a nagy lelkesedést, egyértelműen felnő a régihez (vagy tán túl is nő rajta), de valószínűleg sosem lesz a kedvencem.

RATING: 7.5
This is by far the best dice version of another game I love.

Everything is quite familiar to RFTG: the turn phases, worlds and developments, production and consuption/trading.
The game is definitely very polished, and quite straightforward (at least for players familiar with Race).

There are many different ways to manipulate dice results, so the luck element is quite manageable: it seems it's far more important  to explore into tiles that work well together (like is drawing comboing cards in Race).

There is just a couple of things keeping me from giving an higher rating:
- while Race is a GREAT 2-player game, Roll seem defintely better with more players.
- Race is the greatest game for a 30 minutes time-slot: once the novelty wears off, will Roll be chosen over Race often enough?

Anyway, I am defintely looking forward to some more plays!

RATING: 8
Once we got the iconography down, this game played fast and we wanted to start again. There's still more depth to uncover as our scores have increased with each play. The RNG doesn't seem to be in the dice, since the game gives you so much opportunity to control the dice. Instead, the RNG comes down to the tile pull (though you do get a bit of control over that, too, just less than the dice).  

1/14/17: Ambition has been purchased, but not played yet.

4/7/2017: 12-14 total plays. We're ready to incorporate Ambition.

Edit, June 2017: Finally got around to playing Ambition. Did not use the objectives. The new dice are a welcome addition. The added tiles should keep our interest up in the game, too. The game can exhibit some imbalance if someone has luck with tile pulls and starting tiles. When this edge-case arises the game seems to offer little in the way of combating the resulting synergy, as good strategy on behalf of the unlucky player and/or bad strategy on behalf of the lucky player has little effect on the outcome. The latter situation--when the lucky player makes a few strategy mistakes but not enough to impact the outcome--is especially problematic, as it just drags the game out for the losing party and diminishes a bunch of the game's fun. I'm not sure if that can be fixed on the individual game level due to the randomness between the tile pull and the dice rolling. It's something that likely needs to be lived with. Thankfully (?), the edge case has only happened in about 1 of our approx. 20 games. It's a blip on the radar. Had this happened as one of our first games, this may have greatly impacted our opinion of the game.

RATING: 4
10/16: The dice are an extremely fiddly, uninteresting way to achieve semi-random input for choices. One of the most interesting parts of Race for the Galaxy is looking at what your opponents are doing and trying to predict their action choice. With Roll, your action choice is either easy enough to achieve despite the randomness that there is no point to randomizing in the first place, or it's unpredictable enough that you are essentially playing multiplayer solitaire. Neither of these outcomes is a good argument for using dice. The dual-use tiles are fine. Getting a little more choice is always nice.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I prefer this sequel to its predecessor, "Race for the Galaxy", not just because it uses dice, but because there are piles of dice and they are used in a very clever way to both plan actions and pick an action you want. You start off with some dice manipulation ability from the bat, and during the game get progressively better at manipulating dice, or add a lot more dice to use. The key here is to build your tile tableau engine to be as fruitful as possible on your turn.

Plenty of strategy and intensity in this one. I love most things about this game. My main complaint is that your only interaction with other players comes from figuring out which phases they will activate and using it to your advantage. I'm not sure this is ultimately a bad thing though, as engine building can be very frustrating when other players can easily break what you've built.

Highly recommend this one. As of 1/11/2017, this was once in my top 5 all time, and is currently firmly in my top 20. Doesn't hurt that the wife loves it too.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
So good...

RATING: 9
Initial impression (4 games): This is an excellent game with wide appeal.  It plays quickly, is clever, and yet is very easy to learn.  

Race for the Galaxy is all about figuring out which one or two cards in your hand you are actually going to play, while using the rest to pay for them.  The angst of paying with perfectly good, usable cards is a necessary byproduct of the tough decisions the game presents, but it feels bad.  In contrast, Roll for the Galaxy feels good.  You are at the mercy of the dice, but you have enough power to make just about any roll work for you.  That power feels good.  Getting more dice feels good.  Hearing those dice rattle in your cup feels good.  

This is a game that wants people to like it, and it succeeds admirably without becoming less game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Roll has grown on me. When I first played it I liked how all the game mechanics fit together and the nice decisions about building your tableau and generating enough income, but it just seemed kinda random - too random really. Not so much about the dice; I like the way the different dice work quite a bit. It was more about the tile draws. Sometimes you get nice, synergizing tiles and sometimes you waste turns of exploring trying to find something that remotely works. The card game has the same thing of course, but you cycle *much* more quickly in the card game and see a lot more cards. In Roll, the homeward combo plus your initial tile draws can make a huge difference; good matches and you can just start playing, otherwise you may still be digging for something, anything, after everyone else already has an engine going. I've been saddled with Space Pirates plus Epsilon Eridani a couple times, and it's a terrible combo - no dice into the cup initially, no production world, and a very poor early-game money generation power, when money is so critical. It makes to tough to win. Given all this, I think the explore action is a little pricey and there aren't enough exploration-booster tiles in the bad.

Also, the iconography is not great. After the crisp, clear Race for the Galaxy design, Roll for the Galaxy is a bit of a muddled mess and you end up having to fall back to the text, which (of course) is too small and not readable enough.

Still, I think this can all be true - it probably is - and the game can still be quite fun, even very good - which I think it probably is. Like Race, sometimes you don't get the cards, and it's a game about managing and coping with a quickly-changing playing environment, one where you not only have to manage your own stuff but take advantage of everyone else's actions. The dice management strategies are related to, but quite different from, Race. The game is streamlined, plays quickly and easily, it's great that there is so much simultaneous plotting and so limited downtime (a recurring bugbear for dice games). There is a lot to like. I suspect the balance probably isn't quite where it should be, but it's close enough to make for a fun game.

Strategy Note: Early game income is of course the key. You have to do everything in your power to get some kind of income engine going early, at the expense of any kind of long-term strategy. You can develop strategy once you've got the means to support cycling enough dice to give you options.

Just a random note: Race was really terrific as a 2-player game. Roll is just OK, and is much better multiplayer. 3 or 4 is probably the sweet spot. 5 is fine too.

RATING: 7
Many times, the dice version of a game is obviously just a cash grab on the property, with the dice game being some silly little watered down version of the original.  However, with Roll for the Galaxy, it is evident that much thought went into the game to make it stand on it's own legs, which is great to see!

If you're familiar with Race, Roll has almost all familiar ideas, just implemented in a way that makes the game work extremely well with a dice management system as the main engine.  Many aspects of the game have been cleverly streamlined to allow it to work very well and stand alone, while maintaining all of the familiarity of the original.

If you like loads of custom dice that come together for a very good game, and you like Race for the Galaxy, I don't see much reason you wouldn't like this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
TEXT ON THE CARDS! THANK FUCKING CHRIST!

This game made me realize just how esoteric RftG's iconography really is - because even when paired with explanatory text I still stare at it and my eyes glaze over in confusion.

Anyway - this alone is a huge win and makes the game a lot easier to get into. This captures much of the feel of Race in a pretty substantial dice game. Quite possibly the meatiest dice game I've played. There's lots of good little decisions here - particularly in the double-sided tiles. Good stuff!

Update: It took me awhile to really suss out the strategies here - for a long time I just could not figure out how to manage balance my dice/money economy, but after two wins in a row last night I think I've finally got it!

So yeah it took longer here for the strategy to really click with me, but a dozenish games in now and I think Roll may end up having better longevity than Race. One of the big things is just because of the variety of starting tiles! It's such a small change but it has such a huge impact. And of course, the dice. They've done a really excellent job of balancing the randomness such that it influences the choices you make, without being wholly beholden to your rolls.

I will say this lacks some of the tension I find in Race's cards-as-currency system for me. There's tough choices, but nothing feels quite as agonizing to me as having to discard 2 cards I really want for a planet I desperately need.

Do I like this more than Race now? The answer is... "quite possibly."

RATING: 4

I like that you have to consider what other people are likely to take for their action, but there is a little too much double think there. I often needed to take an action, assumed another player who also needed to would, but they also passed with me in mind. Sometimes people just don't roll what they need and you can't leech so careful thought is often not rewarded, maybe too often than seemed comfortable to me -- Taking into account what dice they have, and how many pips of actions that die has seems possible but beyond the scope of where I want to go in this game. --

In the end, just not the kind of game for me. I still find that Puerto Rico does this kind of action selection better than any other game and in pretty much the same amount of time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Having played RollFTG I have had to decrease my rating of RaceFTG – I’m probably not going to play Race now that I’ve tried this! It’s not that I like Roll for the Galaxy a lot, but it just fixed some of the problems I had with Race for the Galaxy.

It takes care of the fiddly icons; yes, Roll also features a lot of icons, but they are much, much more intuitive, and there is also some text on the tiles which explains what they do! I’ve found that Roll makes more sense and is easier to explain.

Roll is a VERY ”solitaire” game – you really can’t do anything to beat your opponent… This might be a negative for many players, but I found that it was a quite pleasant experience to puzzle with your own player board. The interaction comes in the phase selection where you need to guess which phases the other players are going to activate so you can benefit from that. Cool little mechanism.

It’s a dice game, yes. That means that there is bound to be a certain amount of luck, and yes, you can roll horribly bad and be screwed for a round, but it shouldn’t be too devastating throughout the game – mainly because there are ways to manipulate the dice rolls! First of all you can use any die to activate one (and only one!) phase, it doesn’t have to match. And you can also use “dictate”; i.e. spend exactly one die, which you cannot use this round but will get back for free for the next round, to change any other die to whatever you want. This makes the game flexible and is something I was impressed with during my plays. And even if you do make a crappy roll and cannot activate much, you simply get the dice back for free for the next round, so you get compensated. Oh, and speaking of the dice, I like that there are 7 different kinds and that they have different chances of rolling the different phases. An example; the red dice don’t have any shipping, so if you get a lot of red dice you probably just want to settle a lot of planets.

I also like the various phases, but I especially have to accentuate the explore phase; a very important phase as it’s one of the ways of getting income (which you need to buy dice each rounds) and the only way of getting new planets/developments. And when you draw new tiles from the bag, you can always decide to throw away your old planets in order to draw additional tiles – so even if you got a bad tile, you can always just discard it in the next explore phase if you want. That’s pretty cool!

I’ve played it with 2 and with 4, and both player counts work well! The 2p version has a dummy player which actually works okay, although it’s a little more random compared to guessing which phases the other players are going to activate (because you can make educated guesses)! The game takes about half an hour across the player counts, which is a perfect length in my opinion.

Like Race there are two main strategies: using trade to get VPs (which is a slower strategy but has potential for a lot of points) or rushing to get 12 planets and ending the game before the other strategies start to shine.

RATING: 8
I've described this game as Race for the Galaxy meets Quarriors.  It's a fun, faster-paced (or at least it feels faster-paced) remix of Race for the Galaxy that substitutes cards for oodles of custom dice.

It's a fun game that's gone over well with every group I've introduced it to.  The core mechanics are difficult to explain, and new players definitely feel overwhelmed by the (necessary) explanations of what the six actions do.  But after a few rounds (and rounds go by very quickly) everyone gets the hang of it, and we're off to the Race[s].

The game has enough strategic diversity to keep it feeling fresh.  The high-randomness doesn't feel oppressive, and you get the feeling that the game still rewards strategy and forward-thinking, despite the fact that it's all dice rolls and random tile draws.

The only real down-side is that there is almost zero player interaction, making the game effectively multi-player solitaire.  Race for the Galaxy also suffers from this somewhat.  It's not a deal-breaker, but for players who like interaction this game could feel lacking.

RATING: 6
I'm at odds about whether or not I want to like this game. 

Pros:
Once you get past the initial learning plateau, the game is pretty easy to understand.  An entire round may happen in less than a minute, especially if everyone is familiar with the rules.  

I like the fact that as a development strategy, you have to choose between technologies, settling new worlds, getting money (which helps with the first two), and scoring points to win the game. 

You're constantly wanting to make the best move you can at the mercy of the random die rolls, but fortunately as the game goes on you can increasingly mitigate that randomness and develop a strategy. 


Cons:
For one, the biggest gripe I have is that the player interaction is fairly minimal at best.  Yes, it is pretty much group solitaire.  You can try to predict what your opponents are going to do.  If you're lucky, you'll get additional action types per turn, but that's IT as far as interaction goes.  There's never a need to talk/negotiate/threaten/cooperate at all, you just have to announce what phase the game is currently in.  

The game feels rather short.  The rules specify that the game ends when the victory point pile is empty or one player reaches twelve total tiles in their "tableau."  In my opinion this happens way too quickly.  Sometimes within about 15 minutes.  The player who reaches 12 tiles first is often going to the the winner, even if they mostly ignored collecting actual victory points.  

Overall, I like the fact that there is variety each time you play as you start with a new faction/homeworld, and new technologies/dice to acquire.  

What I don't like is that underneath that superficial layer of randomness, the game isn't all that complex or deep.  It's an OK game, but perhaps it's missing one more layer, like incentivized/cooperative trading between players, an opportunity for military engagements, or actual setbacks challenges to building a space empire. 

As it stands, the game is a nice stepping stone into a larger genre but too simple for me to enjoy consistently.   I'll try the expansion packs

RATING: 7
There are lots of things to love here. It really captures the feeling of RftG, but manages to be its own game. 

Components: The dice are small, but adequate and there are TONS of them in the box. The tiles are nice and thick, but the tile bag is too small. The money markers for the players are somewhat ambiguously shaped, but that just means you get to decide whether yours is a astronaut or a space ship. Love that it includes dice cups (I'm a sucker for dice cups) and that the box is the same dimensions as the Dominion box.

Gameplay: The gameplay is good and pretty easy to understand and teach. The player screen doubles as a player aid and does a good job of capturing all the important rules. The thing I like least is waiting for people to futz with their dice behind their screens. It makes the game go slowly and you constantly need to ask people if they are ready. Turns can happen simultaneously (as in RftG) which is chaotic and makes it feel less like you are playing a game with others and a lot more like you are playing by yourself. This is compounded by the fact that the text on the tiles is small and hard to read at any distance so you can't easily tell what your opponents have going for them. This could be mitigated somewhat by better knowledge of the powers by tile name.

Verdict: Some fun. I'm happy to play, but I'm probably not going to suggest it mostly because of the player downtime and solitaire-feeling gameplay.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
It's hard to grade this game without referencing "Race for the Galaxy." A couple elements hold "Roll" back from the lofty heights of its older brother One is the lack of bonuses for selecting a particular phase. I thought that element added a lot of strategy to "Race" and I feel its absence here. (Though it's worth noting that this - along with a reduction in symbology reliance - make "Roll" and easier game to learn.)

The other issue I have with "Roll" is that I don't dig the premium placed on correctly guessing how your opponents will place their dice. Reading your opponents is a big part of many games and usually I don't mind it because there's usually some other viable path to take if you guess wrong. But here, if you've loaded a bunch of dice in a phase that nobody picks, you're pretty much screwed. String a few wasted rounds like that together and voila! You're out of the game, perhaps well before its ending.

Despite these issues, "Roll" remains a very fun game. If you like dice games and/or don't feel like dealing with the oppressive symbology of "Race," then this might be right up your alley.

RATING: 7
I like this more than the card game. It's been long enough since I played the card game that I can't exactly articulate why. The game just seems to work well with dice, I guess. The only obvious ding is that the game is (still) heavily multi-player solitaire. The most interaction you can hope for is guessing what actions other players will select. But since it's simultaneous, there's a bit of prisoner's dilemma. The only other ding, if you can call it that, is that the game is not auditable by players. Meaning that players have to roll the dice behind their own screen, then arrange them, all of which is done secretly. It would be trivial to change faces if someone wanted to. Meaning that players should not even think about playing this with others who are prone or tempted to cheat, or even with players prone to suspicion of others. It requires complete trust in others. For me that's fine with most people I play with, fortunately.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is a streamlined version of Race for the Galaxy, using dice instead of cards, which is always better! :D

Plus the phases order has been changed, simplifying the acquisition of victory points through production.

Each player roll its dice and sort them behind its screen. Then, he chooses a phase to be played this turn.

After the screens are lifted, all the chosen phases take place, players acquire new tiles, put tiles in play, produce ressources, acquire new dice and gain victory points.
Then another turn begins.

Very fun (more so than the card, in my opinion)!

RATING: 8.3
So much fun to play! Best game of 2015 (so far). I actually like it more than Race for the Galaxy. The dice mitigation mechanisms are very innovative. It doesn't really play like any other dice game I've ever played.

Unfortunately, in my experience (but contrary to the designers'), the produce/consume strategy seems underpowered compared to a simplistic explore/settle strategy. I find that a little frustrating because I enjoy the challenge of finding the worlds that synergize well with each other and gaining and using the appropriately colored dice for producing and shipping to maximize victory points. That just seems a lot more fun to me than just settling a bunch of low-cost worlds as fast as possible, but that simplistic strategy usually wins. I think the game desperately needs an expansion to flesh out and boost alternative strategies.

RATING: 9
2-5 (3)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
(includes Ambition)

After a number of plays, I think this is going to be purged.

In short, I just don't see why I'd play this over Race.  At least Jump Drive (the other member of the Race family) takes 10-15 minutes (though 2PA Race takes maybe 20-25...), while Roll takes the same time as Race.

Further, Roll is very restrictive, by the nature of the dice rolls and also by the limited number of tiles that you see.  That last point is understated, I think, and it took me a long time to see it as the reason I don't like this as much.  The main problem is that, when you ship goods, you get money instead of cards.  In Race, Trading a good could get you a grip of cards, which could either provide you with what you needed, or could give you a whole new direction.  In Roll, you just get money to get your dice back.

With Race, you get two ways to find a new strategy: Explore (esp. Deep Explore) or Consume:Trade.  With Roll, you only get Explore, which itself is far more limiting (you need a lot of dice to see a lot of tiles).  Coupled with the fact that Roll starts you with 3/12 tiles in your Tableau, you are very much steered in one direction and are just hoping that the tiles support that. 

Because of this, Roll games feel very much on rails, but with the off-chance that your plans just won't work due to the roll of the dice.  Race requires far more thought about choosing and pursuing a strategy.

RATING: 8
Man, I just want to eat these dice like they're candy. I imagine the purple dice taste like cotton candy and the green like sour apple and the white like marshmallow. I want to shake them around in my colorful cup and pour them right down my gullet.

The fact that I don't is testament to the fact that I really like this game and that I think rolling these colorful and delicious dice to do neat things like explore planets and develop new technologies and produce weird alien goods is incredibly neat. It plays quickly, has a surprising depth of strategy, and the theme is actually very delightful if you allow yourself to slow down and take a look at what that planet you've been settling actually is. It's low on player interaction but, in a game of this length with such brisk pacing, I don't mind nearly as much as I would otherwise.

RATING: 8
[2015-03-02 First Impression]
Race for the Galaxy is one of my favorite games of all time and somehow I was a little reluctant to get a dice version of it. After reading some reviews and watching [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/video/62923/roll-galaxy/rahdo-runs-through-roll-galaxy]Rahdo's Runthrough[/url], I gave in and aquired a copy. And I'm glad I did.

While my first play saw me struggling more with the system than doing anything to win me the game, my second game already showed me that Roll for the Galaxy contains all the combo building goodness that I like in Race and some more: Roll for the Galaxy features one of the best use of dice I've seen in any game. You have a lot of control over your faith and if you even manage to get a reassign development into your tableau, you can almost always do something good with what you rolled. Brilliant!

[2015-05-11 Rating: n.a. -> 8]
Currently, I do enjoy Race for the Galaxy a tad more than it's dice version because I feel it allows a bit more strategic planning. Nevertheless, I do really like the delicious tactical puzzle posed by the dice. So for now, I'll settle with an 8.
Roll for the Galaxy is just a lot of fun to play and I hope that I can get it to the table a lot in the future.

RATING: 9
I never played Race for the Galaxy but Roll is a brilliant space themed dice rolling game. It combines engine building, future planing, and social deduction (guessing what other's will choose for their phase) in a really tight game. It's my girlfriend's favorite game sofar and is simple enough to teach to non gamers.

Negatives: the symbols are really well done ONCE you know the game, but very confusing for new players. The game can be split into two parts: exploring, developing, settling, versus producing and shipping. If no one else is producing and shipping it can be difficult to go for that strategy.  

I personally love playing because you feel good when you're building your empire and even if you lose you have a good time. I can recognize its flaws but they are easy to move past once you understand the game.

RATING: 6
Fairly fun and I like the shiny dice (although the colours could be better)  but it's not as good as 'Race'.

- There's more luck in 'Roll' than 'Race'. You have the luck of the draw of the tile (as per Race's card draw) but then you have the luck of the roll on top as well.
- People disappearing behind their screens to work their dice out makes it even more multi-solitaire.
- The digging in the bag multiple times in one round breaks the flow up more.
- Some of the tiles seem more overpowered than the cards (particularly the '5' devs).
- There are icons and there is lots of small text.
- Fiddly.
- There is more scope for the players/game to get out of step due to players operating at different speeds - I've been in games where one player has taken his next roll whilst others are still working out what to do with their previous one.
- The theme engagement is at yet one more level of abstraction than Race - i.e. 'Race' is abstractly representing the building and running of a space empire but 'Roll' is just abstractly representing 'Race'. The actions you are taking in 'Roll' only make thematic sense if translated via 'Race'.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

My 2015 game of the year so far.

RATING: 9
Very intelligent re-imagining of Race as a dice game.   At the end of the day, the question will undoubtedly be:  is this better than Race?   Well....   it's certainly longer and more fiddly.   The pace is slower.   But it still gives the same empire-building feel with a lot of interesting choices and a number of interesting strategies to pursue.   The double-sided tiles were an interesting design choice.   The Explore phase feels more complex, while everything else is simpler.   The player screen is really busy, but ultimately very functional and useful.  It definitely left me wanting to play again and again.... and again.   Has proven to be more approachable to those who are lukewarm (or worse) on Race.   Bumping rating up to 9.

RATING: 8

It should be said that, just like Race for the Galaxy, any play with new people is preceded by a fairly hefty rules dump that cannot really be reduced or avoided. It's vital to know all the ins and outs even to start playing, which can intimidate new players - but once they start, they'll quickly get the hang of it.

RATING: 6
I sort of enjoyed this but i think its a hot mess of a game. Its theme appears to be Race for the Galaxy, which on one hand is fine but if you have never played Race this game could feel completely arbitrary. It does have the predictability or the clean but difficult decisions of Race and is even more confusing. On the other hand, i find it more relaxing to play. Due to the unpredictability of dice, the game has even less player interaction than Race. In Race the key interaction was guessing your opponents next move, here it depends what they roll.

I'm not really sure who its for. Race fans might be disappointing as this won't be as competitively sharp. My guess is it exists to be BGG hotness for a while and satisfy people who need that big game of the month to explore the rules of over half a dozen plays and then discard.

If you are looking for a dice game that has more depth (and fiddley complexity) than anything else on the market though, this might be what you are looking for. Most dice games are Yahtzee rehashes this at least tries to be something different.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Muddy art, few interesting strategy decisions and weak theme integration make the game a bland experience that fails to engage the player's imagination. The game overstays its welcome, taking much longer than it should; playing the last few turns just seems like a chore. 

Random factors, especially phase selection, can be annoying and frustrating; I would have preferred a more predictable turn sequence. After rolling dice, you must allocate them to various phases to use them. You can select just one of five possible phases, and you must rely on other players' selections for activating other phases that you might need. As your freedom to allocate is limited, your plans can be frustrated because you can't predict the phase selections of your opponents, and your dice can spend the wasted turn inactive and feckless.

Factions (read: races), developments (read: technologies) and worlds don't add much individual character to a player's empire.  When you get Space Marines, you might think, "Wow, now I'll really have some kind of military power!"  In fact, you just get a red "military" die, which is much like other dice. There are minor differences in the mix of symbols on different dice, but the variations really add very little to game's theme.

Overall, weak theme, lack of engaging strategy decisions and longish playing time make Roll for the Galaxy a game I cannot recommend. Rating: 5.5.

RATING: 7
It took me several plays to warm up this game but I’ve come to like it for what it is – a competitive, simultaneous puzzle race. I mean, I’d rather play Race itself, but this is a more relaxed version where you play against the dice first and the other players second. Player interaction is not absent but it’s not huge either. 

Sure, you can try and deduce what others will do often they don’t have the dice to do it. On many turns, people do things out of synch and finish up long before others because it just didn’t matter. It often feels very much MPS. The big challenge here is optimizing your own powers and dice each turn. And that's fun. When I first played the game, I found this shift in focus lame, but now I can enjoy the game as a plus filler.

Another reason not to take the game too seriously: the possibility to unintentionally knock over one's dice and forget what they were is very high. Just sayin'...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
It's a dice game, but you have a lot of control over "bad rolls".

1. The die distributions are clearly marked on the play aid.  So settle worlds that give you the kind of dice you need.  Furthermore, when you're short on cash, buy back the dice that will suit what you intend to accomplish the next turn.

2. There are no re-rolls.  This is pretty uncommon in dice games.  Instead everybody has a "burn one to reassign one" power, which can be limiting on bad rolls in early turns.  It seems like you want to pick up a Reassign power or two along the way, they've served me pretty well.

I find it easier to "read" other people's intentions in this game, which is useful.  If they have a half-built six-cost, they're probably going to call develop.  If they have a bunch of goods out already, they're probably going to ship.

Also, the roll you call never has to match the face, so you always have one free die reassignment as well.

I feel like I can get more done in a turn in this game than I can in its parent game.

Note, however, that the two player game is much weaker IMO, probably only a 7 or so for me.  It's like playing a three player game where one of the players is a complete moron.

RATING: 7

The developments offer a nice range of effects to help keep your strategies for each game varied and interesting.

RATING: 9
I love playing this game, however the caveat to this is, much like it's predecessor Race for the Galaxy, teaching this game to new players leads to constant rule clarifications. I love the engine maintenance, the managing of income vs points is a constant struggle that takes good planning to payoff. The randomness of which tiles you gain may be the one part that I wish was more manageable (I get points for brown goods, but I can't do anything to assist getting specifically brown planet tiles outside of draw randomly from a bag). It does not dampen my experience enough to harm the game, but it is the one issue I wish could be more manageable.
The issue of dice colour vs tile colour, remembering some colours are wild and when colour matters seems to cause issues with new players and I would rather play with people that know how than teach from scratch. Especially with the full 5 player count.
All that aside, one of my favourite games in the collection and would play whenever possible.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
If you love dice chucking this game is definitely for you! It comes with 111 multi-colored dice and variety of double sided tiles to develop your board that adds different power/abilities/dice. Very easy to store the components in the box and pretty fast setup for a game that has so many different dice and tiles. Fast paced game with simultaneous turn-taking and easy to learn the game. This game is very reliant on players being honest and trusting as the dice rolling and selection is done in secret, behind a player screen that everyone gets. The screen also provides information on the mechanics of the game and how stages are performed. There are many ways to negate the pure luck of dice rolling and there is a good amount of depth in the game. Once you understand how each stage is done and have a grasp of the what the different tiles do and offer, this game goes by very fast and has the perfect length between filler games and heavier euro games. Good game to add to the board game collection!

RATING: 7
Need to play more to confirm my rating - could go up or down. 

It seems to push all the decision making into one part, where you secretly assign your dice.  But since you don't know what others will pick (or even what they *can* pick, because you don't know what they rolled), many of the decisions you agonize over could become completely useless since the phases didn't get picked by other people.

I enjoy games where you have to try to guess what others are picking while you pick them, but this one punishes you even more for guessing wrong, while simultaneously making it even harder for you to guess correctly since the dice, to certain extent, decide what you can choose.

I would like to try it again, but I wouldn't ever choose this over the original.

Menete::
1. Dobsz egy rakat kockával.
2. Szétosztod őket az rftgnek megfelelően fázisokra, de csak egy olyat választhatsz, ami garantáltan végre lesz hajtva. A többinél bízol a többiekben.
3. Végrehajtjátok a kiválasztott fázisokat, amik kicsit mások:
- explore: felépíthető lapkákat (bolygók és fejlesztések) húzol egy zsákból
- bolygót építesz (ez kockák rápakolásával megy)
- fejlesztést építesz
- bolygóra termelsz
- bolygóról eladsz pontért vagy pénzért
4. visszavásárolod az elhasznált kockáidat pénzért. Ez érdekes mechanizmus, mert van olyan kör, hogy akár nyolc kockával is dobsz, de olyan is akad, hogy csak eggyel.

RATING: 6
I like the idea of rolling dice, I like how they are all thematically different. I like how planets give you more dice while developments give you game effects. All good there.

What doesn't work for me is marring all this with the mechanics from Race for the Galaxy. Rolling dice removes or greatly diminishes the amount of control the player has over his strategy when combined with that system. Hence the various ways to mitigate it: reassignment powers, dictate, etc. While they managed to correct that and it is possible to follow a general strategy, it also makes the game extremely fiddly. I would have preferred a streamlined system even if that meant severing the connection to the action selection in Race.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Roll for the Galaxy is a very clever adaptation of the card game. In some ways it feels amazingly close to the original but there are certainly aspects in which both games differ. The dice version is not as interactive, and especially less strategic and more susceptible to lucky draws than its ancestor since the tile churn is much lower than the card churn in Race.

Even though Roll is a pleasant enough game I'm not sure why I would play the dice game in preference to the card game since both also take about the same time to finish.

RATING: 6
I've played 'Race' enough times to know that it's not really a game I want to pursue any further. Confusing iconography, awkward production/trade phases, and a game that rewards experience with bonus card combos means those who have played the game more will always crush those who have played it less while less-experienced players smell defeat about 10 minutes into it. I do like the spirit of Race and the impressive artwork, but that's about it.  I wanted to try Roll because (again) the spirit of Race is pretty awesome and I was hoping for a game that could capture the spirit but does it in a more straightforward way. I had no idea what to expect (or even if this was the goal of the designers).  What I found was a game almost completely devoid of that "spirit". It's quite MPS (with some indirect interaction) and not a very interesting solitaire game at that. Dice are generalized enough that you can pretty much do what you want without the dice getting in the way.  The designers clearly wanted to avoid the dice dictating your turn, so they went for a way to limit that feeling at the expense of the tension inherent in the core mechanic itself.  For a dice game I'm surprised by how irrelevant the dice felt by the end. Never did I feel like I had to make a crucial decision between one choice or the other. The game was all: build tiles, get some dice, roll some dice, build some more tiles, rush to the end of the game.  There are so many points in the tiles and it's pretty easy to build them that pursuing a produce/trade strategy seemed like a comparative time-waster to throwing everything into high-scoring tiles.  Even mining for the best tiles seems like a waste of time as my impression is working with that you got the first time around is slightly better than spending several turns hunting for better tiles. Certainly there are things to explore in the game for a group who loves it and yes there are ways to counter a player who is just dumping everything into colonize/research.  But for a first-time player and one who wants to be inspired to play more, 'Roll' comes off as fiddly, cumbersome, and for the most part, uninspired game.  My '6' rating is based on the fact that it is well-worked, unique as dice games go, and thoroughly designed. I try to rate games less on personal enjoyment and more on the craft of the game itself.  Plus, benefit of the doubt has to go to the fact that I have only played it once.  Lehmann and the Rio Grande team are no slouches when it comes to building something to its completion, and 'Roll' never comes off as half-baked or impractical. My guess is relative newcomer designer Huang did the majority of the design here and Lehmann simply lent his core ideas to the game.  This isn't to say that Huang's design comes off as amateurish (it doesn't) but rather that the game feels less aligned with what I feel most inspired "Race': The theme.  'Roll' literally gets buried under its mechanisms and feels a shade colder because of it.  Though I have never been a true fan of  'Race', I would rather play Race now that I've experienced both.  Honestly, I'd still rather play San Juan.

UPDATE
Great dice version of race for the galaxy. It's different in ways but the feel is very much the same. Many people prefer this game over race and I can see why. The game plays fast with simultaneous selection and is easier to get into than race. It also plays well with 2-5 where as race is more of a 2p and possibly 3p game.

The development abilities are more streamlined and simple. The worlds all do pretty much the same thing, give dice of a color + some bonus.

I haven't gotten the expansion yet but I know it will be a hit. Adds tons more starting variability with home worlds and new abilities. Also the multipurpose dice adds more control

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A streamlined version of the classic Race for the Galaxy. I think the dice made the game more understandable and more "fun", but there are a few shortcomings with regards to Race. First off, a lot of the time when picking a phase you are dictated by your roll. This is not necessarily a problem, but it eliminates a level of strategy compared to Race where it was possible to guess what your opponents would pick and play accordingly. Another problem is that the starting worlds are poorly balanced and some drawn tiles are strictly better than others. The unbalanced tiles/cards wasn't a problem in Race as they could be discarded, but in Roll it's harder to get rid of your tiles.

A small problem I had with Roll was also the plastic cups. It's nice to have bright colours, but the plastic makes far too much noise when rolling.

Adding a comment about differences from Race:
[q="GeorgW"]I'll chime in on the differences between Race and Roll. 

Race plays quicker (perhaps due to my group being more familiar with Race) and has a lot more strategy. While on the surface you make the same kind of choices in both games, such as which planets and developments to go for and which phase to choose, the dice in Roll screw up a lot of the depth in these choices that are still present in Race. 

For example when choosing a planet/development. At any given time in Race you have a hand of around 5 cards. This will of course vary, but in previous turns you've probably also chosen to discard cards that you've deemed to be worse than the cards you kept, so you throughout the game you generally have pretty good control of the cards in your hand. This means that if you play the game well and have reasonable luck, you'll usually be able to find planets and developments that have synergy and will help you win the game. In Roll, maybe you rolled 3 eyes so you looked at 3 tiles and chose 1. Maybe that tile is good for you, maybe not. That's the biggest flaw in Roll to me, because it almost eliminates the engine/tableau building aspect of the game.

A second example is picking which phase you want. In Race, that's a tense choice every turn where you're trying to predict what your opponents will do and balancing choosing something that's better for you or worse for your opponents. It's basically the entirety of Race's player interaction, but it's amazing. In roll, sometimes you roll 5 develops, so you choose develop. The randomness makes the prediction more difficult which in turn ruins the entire point of the mechanic.

With that said, there's still some stuff that Roll does better. It's a much newer game and as such has streamlined the rules a bit and is more colourful. It's also switched the phase sequence in order to make things move a little faster and allow for more explosive turns. 

As a summary, Race is the better game for me, but if you're new to both I'd recommend playing Roll first a few times until you're used to the iconography and flow of the game, then buy Race and never look back.[/q]

RATING: 7
This is a slick design that presents the players with interesting tactical decisions. For me, though, it loses to Race for the Galaxy on almost every level. Roll is longer, less strategic, less interactive, more mechanical, and much more random (with the dice, yes, but especially with the tile draws).

Those who feel that Race for the Galaxy is too unapproachable, too unforgiving, or too cutthroat will probably prefer Roll for the Galaxy. It will also appeal to Feld-ian gamers who want each turn to be a little tactical optimization puzzle.

RATING: 6
It is not a bad game, but clearly an effort to appease the simpletons that "hate" cards and love dice.  So we don't have cards; we have tiles . . .

The selection process is cool except for the 2 player version where an extra phase is rolled AFTER the selection.  This adds nothing to strategy and hurts it more than rolling before, or choosing twice.  Why would the 2 player version introduce a lock factor that ruins playing against your opponent?  I see all positive reviews of the 2 player advanced in Race, sad to see Roll doing the opposite.  

I am sure there is some simple house rules fixes, but I assume anyone that likes Roll better than Race isn't interested in having the best play win.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
The original card game version, Race for the Galaxy was ok but I didn't love it. However I do love this dice version.  It's difficult to pinpoint why, as mechanically they are similar, but I've enjoyed Roll so much I'll give it a try. 

Greater accessibility and little downtime:
Of all my games it's the game I've played the most. In part, because of the simultaneous player actions, it plays very quickly for a medium weight game. I can get at least a couple of games in a session. For a mid-weight game its rare that you get to give the same group a full game to learn and then a full-on more satisfying game in one evening. The quick turns also help new players stay engaged. 
Roll's greatest advantage over Race are that the card/tile powers are spelled out on the card/tile itself rather than relying on iconography. Not having to internalize iconography (like learning a new language) or refer to a overwhelming array of text on a  player aid (like looking up a dictionary) significantly lowers the bar for entry to this game. In Roll you and your opponents tiles and their powers are are always on display; static and easy to quickly view and understand. In Race the information you need to make decisions is more 'hidden' in terms of deciphering iconography but also just physically as your cards are in hand and must be constantly sifted through to see what they do. In short it's much quicker in Roll for the Galaxy to see what you have to play with and make decisions. Its also easier to teach. 

Theme and immersion:
Theme is highly important to my play experience. It makes teaching a game much easier if you can integrate a familiar narrative or theme that rationalizes/grounds the mechanics and immerses players. For instance explaining how the dice in Roll function I might start with- 
"These dice are your contractors for hire and will work in teams to complete projects for you. They come in various colours corresponding to their type of specialization. As individuals your contractors are able to do a variety of jobs but have preferences meaning they like and are better at certain jobs. But as any leader knows a team of individuals don't always do things the things the way you want. So at times you'll have to use your powers to dictate, coerce these dice 'teams' to do your bidding. When they've finished their work they usually 'retire' to the 'citizenry' to spend their hard earned cash. You'll have to pay for them to come out of retirement and reuse them." Managing teams of wayward space mercenaries-for-hire is probably more thematic and fun to explain than managing dice probabilities.

The great tactile elements of roll add to the theme in subtle but satisfying ways. The tumbling possibilities of every round takes off with the deafening rumble of dice being simultaneously rolled in upto 5 player's rocket shaped cups; simulating the roar, uncertainty and excitement felt watching a NASA rocket launch. Constantly moving the dice and tiles around, delving into the big black bag of tiles and shifting the player 'shields' really gets players interacting in the physical space of the game and immersed in their own little piece of this fantastic galaxy. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 1
Almost everything I hate in a box. Fiddly tactical gameplay that sells depth down the river, slapping a half-life on your decisions the minute you make them, while embracing the stupid iconography and unnecessary complexity of Race. Most damningly of all, this game presents a lateral shift, keeping the same playtime while dumbing down everything that made Race special. Its reduced complexity does not reduce its duration. If anything, it makes it longer than its predecessor.

Race is good despite its iconography issues and solitaire aspects because it pairs fast play with surprising depth (though that depth isn't as considerable as Race's champions claim -- it's still a rather light game). Roll doubles down on those flaws and adds a few more.

RATING: 7.25
Dice pools instead of cards, driving acquisition of tiles and Galactic domination. I love the engine behind Race for the Galaxy (RftG), and this is SO close, but...  Roll for the Galaxy is a clever re-implementation of RftG, which changes the flavor of how cash is acquired/used, and the feel of the game, even though the end game conditions are almost identical.  Tiles give powers and or dice. Both are critical as players grow in ability to complete tiles and generate VPs.

For those who love dice rolling, the cups and the custom dice are fun to launch. For those who want their cash resources to remain constant on the other hand, getting used to dice management is different from hand management.

Roll never rated as highly as RftG with me personally, but it's a solid game in and of itself. 

Roll for the Galaxy seems to take a little longer to ramp up the motor to the end game, and at times one can be stuck with insufficient dice to have any positive choices.  Limited dice luck can play a large part in success vs. failure. (Dice HATE Me!)

As big a problem is the reduced flow of available builds- in RftG the Cards fly through, giving many opportunities to build or change strategy; Roll is not as fluid, and the number of tiles drawn much less than the the number of cards in Race, which limits overall choices.  Furthermore the stacking mechanism means it is more difficult to re-prioritize from less desired tiles if a good one comes in, which means luck of the draw bag comes into play. (Tiles HATE Me!)

Race for the Galaxy simply shines brighter as a game- in the end I decided to trade this because I'd rather play Race for the Galaxy.

ロール・フォー・ザ・ギャラクシー

RATING: 10
[center][size=6][COLOR=#878787]LIFETIME GAME [microbadge=28495]:star:[microbadge=28496] "PLAY FOREVER"[/COLOR][/size][/center]

Aspiring interstellar overlords commit handpicked citizens to a mind-warping battle school where they are... [i]changed[/i].  These agents are then dispatched on desperate missions across the cosmos—leading a surgical strike force of war-painted starships, or skin crawling with invisible tattoos that reconfigure into malware under certain wavelengths of light, or their skulls the vessels for a single dangerous idea.

Tomorrow, tomorrow belongs to the Ascendant—and there is no better way to leap for the brass ring than with a boot in your grasping rival's face.

[microbadge=26108]

[i]An unalloyed slam-dunk.[/i]  As a rabid [i]Race[/i] fan I am obviously predisposed to love this, but honestly, [i]Roll[/i] has far exceeded even my naturally high expectations.  Added to everything that's already fantastic about the Mother Game we have a gorgeous feast for the senses—the spray of color, the sound of the dice in the cups, the shaking and slamming, the digging around in that black bag for deliciously textured tiles.

It's a sci-fi jacuzzi with built-in mind-massage attachment.*

[size=7]*Pants optional (but not recommended).[/size]

[b]After 11 plays:[/b]  The response from newbs is overwhelming—all of them have found this far more accessible than [i]Race[/i] proper.  I believe it has to do with the fact that any given [i]Race[/i] game is completely hidden inside an opaque deck of cards, meaning that the game doesn't open up until you're familiar with the contents of that deck, and this requires either a bunch of boring study or many—[i]so many[/i]—crushing defeats.  With [i]Roll[/i], on the other hand, the dice give you a pretty big hint as to what's possible on your turn and so tend to drive play in more obvious directions right out of the gate.

Besides, fiddling around with the dice to "program" your turn feels like you're [i]actually doing something[/i] rather than just looking at a bunch of cards and having no real idea of what to do next.

P$.  They're not "dollars", goddammit—it's pronounced "galacticred" and each one respresents the economic output of an entire solar system across 31.5 megaseconds.

Includes:  [thing=175754]Ambition[/thing]

[size=7][b]A Very Important Note on Space Marine Recruitment:[/b]  As the recruitment protocols are a preëxisting component of the drinking water on most civilized worlds, the consumption of them constitutes retroactive consent.  This is only important for the recruit's relatives as the recruits themselves are neurologically predisposed to be "just fine" with the sudden change in life-trajectory.  [i]Oorah![/i][/size]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Race is the better game. Roll is the better experience. Dice are fun but their function as a way to limit your actions almost disappears entirely after 1 or 2 rounds. What you're left with is a mechanic that feels more like a selling point rather than a compelling obstacle. To get a bit more personal, the amount of plastic contained in this game is over-abundant. When you use less than half the components in every game, that's a poor product design (not poor game design). This game should have been an app. The reliance on gamer etiquette, superfluous upkeep, and plentiful component interaction are all things an app version shines at. 

+Fun engine building game 
+Quality components and art
-Reliance on etiquette
-Some design elements are a waste

RATING: 7
DICE GAME
Etter å ha sett litt flere anmeldelser og spill har jeg virkelig fått lyst til å prøve dette spillet. Virker raskt og har enkle regler samtidig som man må planlegge og ha en strategi. Alike vel har spillet lite interaksjon mellom spillerene utenom at man kan henge på hva den andre spilleren prioriterer som sin hoved-handling. Faller kanskje litt i samme kategori som Castles of Burgundy, men med helt andre spillemekanismer.
Kan virkelig være noe for oss hvis vi har lyst til å prøve et spill i denne kategorien.
Har også en utvidelse - Roll for the Galaxy: Ambition - som bl.a. innfører mål/oppdrag i tillegg til to nye kategorier av terninger og en hel del nye teknologi- og planet-kort.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Ordinarily not a fan of dice games, and I really like the original Race for the Galaxy, so I was highly skeptical of this.  However, I found that there are enough things you can do to mitigate the randomness of dice that I didn't feel totally at the mercy of probability.  The game is much, MUCH easier to teach than Race, because there's no need to memorize all the little symbols (most of which I found intuitive, but many others did not agree).  As a result, I feel like I can play this with new people and we can have one game in which they sort of learn and then immediately play a second game in which they understand what they're doing.  Race has a much steeper learning curve.  I was pleasantly surprised by how much I enjoyed this one.

RATING: 9
Instantly fell in love with this. Remarkably easy to understand all the things you can do, which is a considerable amount, and just works like a well-oiled machine. Glad I picked this up and hope it is well-supported with a good flow of expansions over the years. 

Has gone over well with everyone I've introduced it too and is so much easier to teach than Race for the Galaxy, its predecessor. I only wish it had as much, or more, content than Race. There has only been one expansion since it released and it's been quiet with no news of another in sight. Please don't let this one die, it's very highly ranked and sold well, I'm sure. I just want more tiles for the bag!!

Update - 8/28/17 - Still nothing has been announced since Ambition's release, which is a real shame, because I feel this game surpasses Race for the Galaxy, in terms of being more approachable to new gamers, since Race is much harder to explain and a more experienced player will trounce newbies. It irks me so much that the games I love don't get expansions(well more than 1), but this game has a good amount of replay just with the base and Ambition.
