RATING: 7
It's a very cool dice game. Dice have symbols that let you do different actions (six different types) and the different colored dice have different ratios of the symbols. Over the game, you'll be building an engine to acquire better dice and build tiles that will help you score points, money, or give you more flexibility placing dice.

...and then at the end of the game, you realize you might have just played the whole game completely absorbed in the game. There were four other people at the table too? Oh yeah, I guess there were.

While there are some subtleties where you don't want to have your nose completely buried behind your screen, the experience overall if quite solitaire. Like... the most solitaire board game I've ever played. It leaves me with some mixed feelings.

RATING: 7
For veteran Race players the essence of this game is the same as before, there are two strategies: the settlement rush and produce/ship. You choose one and hope to draw the tiles to match your strategy. Those same veterans will also find that more has changed than they might expect from reading the rules. On the one hand much has been simplified: there are no distinctions between production/windfall worlds or military/non-military worlds. On the other, there is even more luck. You may want to settle, but if you don't get the dice, you can't and for the same reason it's harder to 'leech' off another player's action choice. This strikes me as going in the wrong direction, there's little to distinguish one world from another, while many developments simply allow you to 'reassign', i.e. use a die for an action other than the one rolled. This makes the game feel both more bland and more random, not a great mixture. It also needs 3 players, I'm no fan of the 'roll a white die to choose a third phase' rule in the 2 player version, it introduces a chaos element that can decide a close game. This is the weakest game yet in the Race universe, that doesn't make it bad, just not as great as you might expect.

RATING: 7
A mildly entertaining, passable game. Unfortunately, I find that it lacks interesting decisions. The tiles you randomly draw pretty quickly determine what strategy you're going to use for the game (and there are really only two general strategies: build lots of developments and worlds or ship lots of goods), and for the rest of the game you're just thoughtlessly following that strategy. For me at least, there just aren't enough options. Every game seems to end up becoming a series of easy choices that have essentially already been made for you based on what tiles you happened to start with. This doesn't make for an exciting game, and often times the winner seems to be whoever starts with the best combo of tiles.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Roll. Them. DICE!

A great economic engine building game that uses dice as the pieces for that engine. The artwork and dice and tiles are incredibly aesthetically pleasing, but lying beneath the surface is a very quick, engaging and deceptively deep game experience. The use of dice to determine where workers will be placed in a given round is intuitive to grasp and great fun, but the kicker is that the luck of the dice is able to be mitigated by correctly anticipating how other players will be utilizing their own dice in constructing their engines. This is a great design, and a lot of fun to game around. Games are incredibly quick and competitive. The combos of tile layouts and strategies are quite diverse given how many different possibilities there are, and new, more efficient ways to victory are constantly emerging, making this game quite addicting in the best way. 

Full disclosure: I never win this game. Ever.

*Edit* I won!

RATING: 8
I was very suprised when I first heard about this game, I thought that the original cards game was perfectly fine (except for one of the expansions). And then a friend told me that was a dice game and I was even more shocked, but the truth is that the game is amazing! It is really fun and the dice/workers mechanic works amaizingly! I am not a huge fan of games in which rolling dice is the core mechanic but, this one, is brilliant. It takes more or less the same time to play than the original, but even less complicated as you do not have to shuffle hundreds of cards. The cards have been replaced by tokens and all are kept in a bag, so shuffling is extremely easy.

Seasoned veterans of Race for the Galaxy might not like these simplifications, but Roll for the Galaxy is the answer for those who have wanted to enjoy the original game but found it to have too steep of a learning and mastery curve.

RATING: 7
2015-02-20

Initial Rating: 7.0 (February 2015)

For me, Race for the Galaxy is one of the best games ever made. Since the beginning, it had has a permanent spot in my Top Ten favorite games, so I had huge expectations from Roll for the Galaxy, after I played it, I have some mixed feelings for the game, but the overall feeling is that I like it.

If you are familiar with RFTG, the game explanation is a breeze and can be done under 5 minutes, if you have never played RFTG, it still seems the game is easier to teach than its parent game which is a huge plus. The art is very cool (identical to RFTG) and the playtime is around 1 hour but I think it can be reduced a lot by experienced players. The game offers interesting decisions (where to play my dice, should I dictate? Which die to use when doing X action, How many tiles to recycle when scouting, should I play a world or a development?) and the dice are good looking. 

Roll for the Galaxy comes with a great player aid and the components are of good quality. I find the iconography to be very intuitive but I also have the same feeling for its parent game and many friends disagree with me here :D.

The game flows easily, each turn players roll the dice in their cups and then they plan according to what they rolled. You can choose to play any phase with any dice but from that point onward the dice need to match the phase you want to play, so there is some luck in this, I’m still not sure how much the luck factor affects the game. Some dice are better to settle while others are better to develop etc. I really like that.

Bottom line, Roll for the Galaxy does have the same feeling that RFTG has. I feel a seasoned RFTG player has an advantage in this game as well. Most of my friends prefer this new implementation but for me and my girlfriend, it doesn’t even come close to replace RFTG. As it stands Roll for the Galaxy is a borderline 7.0 for me and with more plays its rating can go either way.  I’m still not sure how much the luck factor affects the game and I think Roll for the Galaxy requires less skill to play well. For now I will keep the game and I want to play it again soon.

Current Rating: 7.0

RATING: 8.5
My enjoyment of this game has grown with repeated plays.  One must chose the tiles they will develop and worlds to settle carefully.  Don't just settle on your draw.  You must make sure there is synergy between your worlds and your developments to build your engine.  Then comes the pivotal moment when you transition from building your engine to using your engine. Knowing when your engine is "good enough" is key.  The follow mechanic is a key nuance in this game.  You must not only have a good strategy, you must be able to read your opponent.  What action does he need to take?  Will he gamble that someone else will initiate it?  For example, is he willing to gamble on you initiating production so he can focus on shipping?  Although this incredible game is not very complex, it would not be recommended as a gateway game.

RATING: 8.75
I didn't have the best history with Race for the Galaxy... When I first played it, my wife wasn't a fan, which pretty well killed any chance to get deeper into it. And my subsequent plays were with a couple that had played it extensively. It was one of those games that really needs to be played with opponents of your own skill level, and unsurprisingly I was soundly thrashed as they were completing their tableaus as I was beginning to figure out how this whole production and shipping thing worked... Despite this, I was still remarkably interested in Roll for the Galaxy, and its roll selection (zing!) mechanics.

My interest was warranted as it turns out, I'm really quite enamoured with Roll for the Galaxy. While it seems as though it may lack the depth of its predecessor, there are still plenty of options to consider given that each of those numerous tiles is double sided, and can be placed as a development or planet. And the variety of starting tiles and homeworlds combinations give each game a little bit of different flavour, while providing you with an initial strategy to pursue. And I've seen a variety of successful strategies end up winning the game; whether it's a small tableau with a focus on consuming as quickly as possible, or a settlement strategy that attempts to pour planets into the tableau, it's nice to know that there's not one preferred way to win the game. It's satisfying to build your production engine, and when you factor in the the chance to read your opponents' minds and draft additional actions on your turn, it adds a nice deduction element as well.

There is a double dose of luck in the game however. Since it is a blind tile draw, there is always the chance that someone is going to stumble into pulling the exact tiles they need at the exact time they need them. In one game, I was fortunate enough to pull two 6+ development tiles. While it took a while to get both into my tableau, the points plus bonuses they provided helped me easily win the game by a significant margin. Meanwhile, my opponent was stuck pulling low to middling tiles that were providing him additional income, but little else. This is at least a little mitigated by the fact that you can choose the orientation of the tile and place it into the stack as a development or a planet, but the luck element is still there. And secondly, as expected with a game of this nature, the dice have a huge luck factor as well. I have had games where I have been absolutely unable to roll what I needed to progress my civilization. The Dictate action thankfully provides you the ability to reassign a die to whatever action you need, but you will still feel a little hamstrung when you need to assign multiple dice to an action to proceed with your strategy and are only able to commit one each turn. Mercifully, because this plays so quickly, your torture will be brief and it is remarkably easy to re-set and play another game.

As it stands, I haven't ever had a solitary game of Roll for the Galaxy; they always end in a rematch - which to me says a lot about the quality of this game. When your engine is just about to get humming, and someone pulls the last VP chip or throws out their twelfth tile and it comes crashing down, there's an immediate desire to run it all over again and see if you can do better. I can't predict how this game will age; there's a chance that the luck factors will become too burdensome over the course of multiple games. But as it stands right now, the brilliance of the other aspects more than make up for the shortcomings, and I am really looking forward to playing more.

RATING: 6
RFTG is one of my favorite card games. Given this and the hype on release, Roll for the Galaxy was a disappointment on its first outing. Strategically, there’s nothing new to be found here although it’s as complex and deep as a dice game could get. The randomness of the cards was replaced with the randomness of the tile draws. In fact, it’s more random as less tiles are drawn. On top, the dice introduce more randomness. The mitigation mechanism of reallocating dice is a crutch. Handling many small dice behind a screen, attributing different symbols, reallocating them are all inelegant and intransparent mechanisms. I would play again but I see no reason to play this instead of the card game so far.

RATING: 9.3
I've played this over 100 times and I'm almost always itching for another game. Huge replayability. Some house rules I like.

-At the start, draw two of each starting tiles for your tableu and pick the combination you want.

-End the game after 18 tiles are placed and increase the victory point chip win condition to 18 VP chips per player in the game.

Some say that the randomness of dice rolling takes away from the strategy in the game. I couldn't disagree more. Unless you're playing a perfect information strategy game there will always be an element of randomness. In this game, the very large random element that dice present is mitigated by basically giving you one completely wild die each time (of your choosing), giving everyone an early game mechanism to change another die to anything you want, AND putting in MANY tiles and abilities that let you control die faces in very fun and interesting ways. If you have a cohesive and thought out strategy, it is viable and can lead to a win. Super well balanced. I feel like instead of coming out with an expansion to fix some problems with race for the galaxy, instead, they reinvented it and improved on things that were less than ideal in race. Brilliant execution.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
wowowowoww this may be a contender for the best 2-player game I own so far, with patchwork just slightly ahead because of its' accessibility. It also scales really well. I love the simultaneous play aspect of the game, although the gameplay runs the risk of a multiplayer solitaire feel. I like but don't like that we base the game off the honor system. Its easy to accidentally knock a die over when moving pieces

RATING: 8
I'm one of those players who prefer Roll over Race for the Galaxy. The dice mechanics are more elegant and I have consistently come up with strategies that actually worked out playing Roll while I am terribly bad at Race, because card games hate me.

However, I do understand that Race is not a bad game. I just prefer Roll for the Galaxy because I understood this game faster than Race for the Galaxy, which still makes me slow down and rethink my cards because it never seems to really click with me.

I guess having actual credits instead of discarding cards to pay for is the one huge difference for me. If Race had a similar mechanic instead of discarding cards... but I digress, wouldn't be the same game I suppose or unbalanced or whatever.

Love it, especially with the Expansion "Ambitions" which adds more layers to the overall strategies. Would roll with it anytime.

RATING: 7
Initial rating based on solitaire play to learn the game.  Seems easier and quicker to play than Race.  The unofficial solitaire variant is a good opponent.  Still very much like Race in that there isn't a lot of time to build a great engine, you need to monitor your opponent and modify your plans to stay competitive.  

The use of separate tiles for the developments and planets and dice for goods/workers relieves some of the decision making in Race about which cards to use as good or to pay for developments or planets is gone.  I like that change.  This seems like a really good mid-complexity Euro.  No direct interaction, but your actions and choices are definitely influenced by other players.

[size=12][b]Why I like Roll for the Galaxy:[/b][/size]
+ There does not seem to be a dominant strategy. I often go for exploring + researching but have lost and won using this strategy. 
+ I like the mechanics of the game
+ There is high player involvement
+ There is decent replayability
+ The game is easy to teach
+ The duration is good
+ The game requires meaningful decisions to win the game and there are some ways to mitigate bad dice rolls. 

[size=12][b]Fixable dislikes in regards to Roll for the Galaxy:[/b][/size]
/ People could cheat. The solution here is to not play with cheaters. 

[size=12][b]Why I dislike Roll for the Galaxy:[/b][/size]
- I have not played Race for the Galaxy and that might significantly change my review and rating. 

[size=12][b]VERDICT:[/b] Sometimes willing to play this game[/size]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.7
Okay, so I played Race for the Galaxy long, long time ago and back then I did not like the experience (especially compared to San Juan). I had so many problems with it that ever since I did not even feel like giving it another try. Yes, I know it starts to shine after five or ten plays but... no, I did not feel like I want to experiment with it further. But as I like dice games I wanted to give Roll for the Galaxy a try.

I'm not even sure about all the details of the original rules anymore, but I do like many of the differences I noticed. First, the dice part - I think it's clever, allowing quite a bit of freedom but still randomizing your actual 'hand' you can do things with (also, feeling a bit like a deck builder where you choose which cards you want to draw from your deck). I also like that while seemingly the tiles offer fewer different actions than the cards did, what these tiles offer are clearly written on the tiles so even as a beginner I do not have to refer to the rulebook all the time. I like the two-sided tiles that make the possible "shuffling through the deck, looking for certain cards" part of the original just not work here. Also I think it was a wise thing to change the order of the actions this time (first produce, then ship) as otherwise this game would have lasted longer than necessary for a dice game.
So, all in all, I like this one and probably because of the dice I like it more than I liked San Juan. (I guess I'd even buy it if it were released in Hungarian, although the price is very high.) What's more, it might work as an icebreaker for me: now, after more than five years I'm thinking of giving Race for the Galaxy another try.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I might bump this game up to an 8 eventually, but I've been saying that a lot with Euros and after repeated plays they just start feeling like the same old thing again. Roll is definitely a lot easier to learn than its predecessor, as all the effects are written out clearly besides the iconography. Also the guessing of what action your opponent is going to take felt a lot more important (whether it actually is or not) because you could have a lot of dice be not utilized if you e.g. roll a lot of settle symbols but choose the produce action hoping someone else chooses settle that turn so you can do both. The way you get your tiles to build is neat but I'm not sure if I like it more than Race's cards. I wish you could see more tiles per explore as unless you are willing to abandon previous ones you can only see one per turn, and trading goods no longer lets you see more cards. It's kind of cool how the tile queue works and the double sided nature of the tiles, but it seems to make getting specific tiles a lot harder. Also, playing expensive tiles seems a lot harder in Roll because you need to potentially lock multiple dice out of play for multiple turns to pay for the tile.

I seem to generally not like dice allocation games, but this one I didn't seem to really have a problem with and I actually kind of enjoyed working with what you have, as even if you made poor rolls you at least don't have to pay to return the dice back, and there's some neat management of how many dice you can afford to lock at a time to advance your production/development/settling versus actually needing something to keep your economy afloat for the next round.

Starting with two tiles with one of them being a double tile makes your starting stuff feel much more different than anyone else, and I think that is one thing that this game did much better than Race.

Overall I think I still like Race better, but I think they still feel different enough from each other, and if I could get this game in a trade I think it would be nice both as a spin off and as a way to help people learn to play Race.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Roll for the Galaxy takes the essence of Race for the Galaxy (one of my all-time favorite games), removes the huge learning/teaching hurdle, and adds similar-yet-new-and-fresh-enough mechanics to make it a unique, excellent experience.  Different enough to justify having both games, but due to the intuitiveness and ease of learning, it has all but replaced Race for the Galaxy, as far as games that actually hit the table go.

RATING: 8
My absolute favorite dice game, Roll for the Galaxy takes the concepts presented in Race and just does them way better. I love the way that each die is used as an action, but you have to cycle them fully back through. A lot of people underutilize the ability to put tiles back in the bag, something that addresses most peoples' major complaint about Race, because there's so much flexibility in the strategy you take. Ambition is a phenomenal expansion, adding two new dice types and objectives that can help steer you towards a strategy early on.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I was curious about Roll for the Galaxy since it was announced, but also wondering how it was going to improve on Race for the Galaxy.

I played it a couple of times at the con and it definitely has a cool "feel" to it. There's no doubt that rolling those dice or adding new dice to the cup is a lot more satisfying than drawing cards.

On the other hand, it feels a lot more speculative then Race for the Galaxy because you end up placing dice in actions that you hope other people will call. That didn't feel like a satisfying mechanic to me. I know it's somewhat present in Race, but in a much smaller way and you're not actually gambling with your resources (dice) like you are here.

In the end, it felt like a slower game than Race and didn't add anything past the dice novelty. I'd be sticking to Race in the future.

RATING: 10
A medium weight game with universal appeal, lots and lots to explore, and beautiful components. It just feels *good* to play.

It replaced Race for me because it is far easier to teach, and the components have an immediate visceral appeal even to non-gamers. It has made many fans among my friend group, even among players who don't enjoy complicated space games, because they simply love the way it looks and feels to play.

It is debatable which game is better, but Race is probably a more intricate puzzle with more variety in engine building and draw variance instead of dice roll variance. (Race is just a pain to teach to people who are turned off by card games that appear complex.)

RATING: 9
One of the best tableau building games.  It has a light space theme but works very well with the mechanics.  You are exploring, building developments, settling worlds, trading and consuming goods and producing new goods.  Cards function as money as well as having powers.  You have hard decisions on what to save and what to keep. 

The biggest issue are the iconography and the amount of time it takes for a new player to learn.  Once you know the icons well, the game plays in 30 minutes.  It is immense fun with experienced players.

RATING: 8
SUSD review sold me on this. Random chance of dice rolls is supplemented by being able to change them for a cost of another die. The game is about as random as what cards you draw in the original, with you being able to figure out strategies to make due with what you get. Very neat and every new development/planet tile let's you use more dice, dice in a different way, easier costs to getting the dice back, etc. Even easier to teach than Race, and even better if they've played Race. Likely will never completely replace Race, but it's definitely targeted for a slightly less cerebral experience.

Bought off Amazon. The Guardtower was a full $60, and just wasn't wanting to spend extra $20 to support them.

RATING: 10
I love this game!  My current favorite dice game.  We will see if it is just a reaction to "the new hotness."  The game may have limited amounts of player interaction, but the interaction it does offer is brilliant.  It will take a new player about one game to figure out what is going on.  I have introduced this game twice.  Each time after the first game I was asked to play the game again.  I find this to be the calling card for a fantastic game.  If you know how to play and are "the teacher" in your group, you may want to sit out and coach the other players during a learning game.

RATING: 8
The news of a reimplementation of one of my favorites as a a dice game initially did not make me sit up and take notice – I don’t feel that I’m anywhere close to exhausting its depth or appeal even after a couple hundred games, and retooling a game to dice usually means simplifying it considerably.

The early comments here on BGG were a first hint that I might be wrong about this, reading the rules strengthened that feeling, and finally playing it convinced that Roll for the Galaxy is a very good game in its own right. While it does not allow for as much strategic planning as Race (and the constant decisions connected to your strategy: what card to play, what to save for later, and which ones to pay for the useful ones), it compensates for that with delicious tactical puzzles posed by your dice. Apart from the ingenious rework of the turn structure, keeping intact all the risks and rewards of leeching from the other players, there are lots of smart little decisions that create both smooth gameplay and a vast decision space. The two-sided Dev/Planet tiles with their balanced sum of cost and the build stack are examples of this.

All in all, I’d recommend giving this one a try to every fan of RaceftG. One caveat: I can’t properly judge how accessible this game will be to anybody not acquainted with the original, but I can’t believe that it will be an easy ride.

RATING: 10
This one was a surprise for me.

While I understand the mechanics and subtle nuances with Race for the Galaxy, I've always found Race to be strictly a two player game.  With the numerous icons and heavy decision making, all it takes is one player or two players to take their sweet time to ruin Race's fast paced gameplay hence the preference for a two player as opposed to a 3 or 4 player experience.  The problems with Race is further compounded by the steep difficulty curve with the various symbols to understand and interpret for a new player

Roll for the Galaxy fixes many of the issues I've had with Race while maintaining the engine building and puzzle solving of the original daddy.  While completing a game can take a bit longer than regular Race, I'm willing to accept a longer game if it's easier to teach and less AP-prone.

Gone are the awkward icons replaced with text at the bottom.  Furthermore, each development and world do only ONE thing, as opposed to several things at once.  Planets will always give you a die, while Developments gives you a new ability to work with your engine building.  Another major improvement is that all tiles, which replace the cards, are double sided.  One side is always a Planet and the other side is a Development.  This means whenever you draw tiles, you can always get something useful.  If you don't like your tiles, you can exchange existing tiles you own with tiles in the bag, thus giving you some form of mitigation.

Another improvement is paying for things.  Instead of using cards in your hand, you get "space bucks" that allow you to buy discarded dice back into your cup to use for the next turn.  This eliminates a potential AP behavior of deciding which cards to discard and simply have the player buy whatever dice they need.  Sure, it removes much of the depth of the game, but it also makes the game faster and more enjoyable for everyone at the table.  

There are more things I like about this game but those are the major highlights for me.  To me, this is one of the best engine builders out there that I prefer more than the original Race for the Galaxy or behemoths like Terraforming Mars.  There is some player interaction in here, but they are subtle like a game of Gin Rummy as opposed to in-your-face interaction like Blood Rage or Kemet.  Definitely one of the bigger highlights in 2017 for me.

RATING: 8
Pros:

--Beautiful components. The gorgeous tiles make building your tableau a joy.

--Simultaneous action resolution. Keeping downtime low increases the fun factor immensely.

--Multiple, engaging strategies without being too mathy. 

--Luck mitigation. While it's a dice game, there a million ways to alter your dice and reduce your bad luck - in fact, doing so is the chief tactical component of the game.

--That addictive magical something that keeps you coming back for one more turn or one more game.

Cons:

--A bit more of a non-intuitive learning curve than most light games have - while it's a reasonably light game, it's not a gateway game.

--Some strategies seem a bit better than others - it seems generally easier to win through building developments than by consuming goods.

RATING: 8.5
This is an excellent reimagining of Race For The Galaxy. I don't feel like the game is "better" than Race as it's really its own thing. In any case, it's an extremely well-done and engaging mapping of some of the core Race concepts onto a different style of game. In fact, the game feels quite unique. The combination of role selection and worker assignment works great. I also like how there's an element of building up your work force (dice/pool building) alongside the usual Race-style special powers for building up your tableau.

All in all, an impressive piece of game design. My compliments to Wei-Hwa Huang and Tom Lehmann.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Has obvious similarities to Race, but feels different enough to warrant owning and playing both. A few differences:

- In Race you're seeing loads of cards and deciding which to play and which to sacrifice. That doesn't happen in Roll. Instead, you draw double-sided tiles (Worlds/Developments); when you explore, you may discard X tiles, then draw (and keep!) X+1 tiles.

- Consume happens after Produce. This is significant.

- No icons to memorize.

Overall, Roll is more beginner-friendly. The randomness of the dice rolls isn't too bad because there are ways to mitigate it. 

Plus, you get roll cupfuls of dice and slam them on the table, so that's good.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Spiel 2015

Wow, I really like this game! We had to think a little about our first turns on how everything works but after that you really get intrigued by how you can manipulate the dice, getting them from your citizenry into your cup and on the board. I personally like it more than the card game at the moment - at least it's easier to get a grasp on the game than the card game and it has dice - a lot of dice! Would love to play ANYtime. Not much downtime either with more people as everyone plays simultaneously. The dice mostly add randomness but don't make it a luck-fest as you can always select the phase that you want and with the dictate action, you can re-arrange another die, not including the re-arrangements from cards in your empire. 45-60 minutes is easily within reach for most people. The Ambition expansion is also interesting to add to the base game, adding orange and black dice. We haven't played with the ambition tiles yet though. Try to fit all components in expansion box.

*** TOP 5 games of all time ***

RATING: 8.65
I liked it but I didn't feel in LOVE at first play...
My main "problem" with the game is one that I had anticipated before, the rolling and assigning the dice in secret... for me it takes a bit of the enjoyment I get from games, I like to see people making their decisions and thinking along and in this game that just isn't possible.

Also, the possibility of making mistakes during this phase is real and those can only be caught in the next phase and by then it will be impossible to correct them without the secrecy of that phase and what all players have done being compromised...

I also don't usually enjoy games where everyone does everything at the same time, like I said, I like to see the game evolve and see how others think and plan their moves, and once again here this can happen and it's encouraged by the rules and I don't like that... and when I play I'll do the the actions by faction tile order, I know the game will be longer but that's the way I like to play games... if it's to be playing by myself I'll just turn on my xbox...

Despite all that, I liked the game so, there's a good chance I'll like it more with more plays

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Feels like a slight improvement over Race for the Galaxy. More streamlined, more ways to mitigate crummy draws, due to each tile having two sides, and the fact that the explore action lets you chuck unwanted tiles to draw extra tiles. Still, I never was a big fan of Race for the Galaxy. Both are okay games, but I just don't get what the hype is all about, even after numerous plays of both games. It's fun to try to craft a strategy out of the cards/tiles you draw, but I get bored with doing that after a couple plays. Also, it just feels like the winner is the one who got better tiles. Yes, there is a fair amount of skill involved in deciding how to play the tiles you got, but among equally skilled players, it ends up just being a crap shoot. And it's not at all uncommon for a lesser skilled player such as myself to draw a great set of tiles that basically drops a winning strategy right in their lap. That's just a tad more randomness than I tend to enjoy. It's fun for a couple/few occasional plays, then I'm done with it.

RATING: 8.3
Very good take on the original card version. It feels like it manages to reach the same depth than it's older brother with a lot smaller learning curve. RATING: 9
In the dice version of this game, players roll dice to determine their actions. The dice have the symbols to explore, develop, settle, produce and ship. Different colored dice have more of a certain attribute. You slect a role you want, and you are allowed one non matching dice symbol to be assigned to that role, and further you may "dictate" a futher dice, so you could get 3 dice for that action. 

Instead of cards, the game uses 2 sided tiles which are either settle planets or develpments on either side and you set into your mat to construct.  The shipping action (trade or consume) is moved last into the phase unlike the main game, and when you trade, you use a marker to track the money, rather than use cards in the original game. You then use your money to buy your available dice to use. 

I really like this game. It reminds me of the fun I had when the orginal game came out. You want to get familiar with all the tiles and there are different strategies to pursue. There are 9 starting tiles and 9 double tiles that are dealt out, so you have a wide variety of combinations of starting home worlds. It's clean, and games do clock in at 45 minutes once you get used to the system. I want to get in a ton more games to look at all the tiles.

RATING: 7
Race for the Galaxy redone: not a new game per se. Overall there is fun aspects from the original, but the new 'improvements' are not great. It is worth a little extra time investment in the original. 

PROS:
-Game is promoted as a more streamlined than the original. It is, but definitely not significantly so. 
-Windfall planets are removed, which is ok (windfall was an extra layer of complexity). 

CONS:
-Consume has been replaced with ship, which I would argue is more complicated (also convoluted). 
-The option to explore for 5 cards (now tiles) and keep one is no longer available, which is bad; limits combinations and introduces more luck of the draw. 
-The dice dictate what you do more than I like, and makes it incredibly difficult to identify what role others will chose. 
-Dice influencing my decisions takes away skill. 
-More of a solitaire game than the original (which already is quite solitaire based).  
-Game ends abruptly (more so than the original) as you start with a large double tile (1 world and 1 tech) and another world. Thus start the game with 3 tiles in your tableau opposed to 1, and they are randomly selected!

RATING: 3
Replacing colourful, well-illustrated cards with tiny, uninteresting dice is never a good thing, so I didn't expect to like Roll For The Galaxy. It was far worse than I had imagined.

Although people have said that Roll For The Galaxy is easier to learn than Race For The Galaxy, the mystified expressions on the faces of several savvy gamers during our initial play spoke otherwise.

Gone are Race's standardized results and bonuses for the various phases chosen, replaced instead with variable results based on the number of phase symbols on dice rolled. This causes a huge amount of chance to be infused into a game where previously there was none; not an improvement when the dice now block you from taking meaningful action, round after round. Also, the conversion of cards to dice meant that the game's currency converted from cards, held in one's hand that displayed the possibility of future turn actions in an eye-catching manner, to dice whose utility needs to be calculated by understanding the chance of certain symbols being rolled.

With this reduction of currency to a strictly monetary value, the designers implemented a recycling program for the dice, rather than them just be expended as cards were in Race. This added an unnecessary economic element on top of an already complicated and iconographically dense game.

So, Roll For The Galaxy is a bland-looking, overly complicated, chance-dominated game that is maddeningly inferior to its predecessor. I can only hope that this piece of garbage was created to gather funds for a streamlined second edition of Race For The Galaxy.

RATING: 9
This is not an average "dice conversion" game. It inherits most of the mechanisms from the card game and create some new compelling and smart ones to introduce the dice. It succeeds at preserving most of the feelings of the original game and not just some "marginal connections".

These mechanics create a globally consistent game that is easy to understand and coherent at the same time. The symbols are easier to understand; RftG fans will make the jump very easily. 

Usually dice games do not feature choices beyond how much you "push your luck". This is not the case. There are plenty of choices to be made here. Still, the game is incredibly short. Veterans can play it in 30 minutes or less. All actions are carried on simultaneously, that means there's no time between turns: you are playing during every minute the game lasts.

I have some criticisms though:
-> The initial factions will condition your strategy.
-> It's not as "easy" to guess your rivals' actions (now they depend on what they need, but also on what they roll; you might know the first, but not the second).
-> You can have runaway players in some game, but they get an early good combo. It's almost impossible to catch up, and even if the games are not long, they do not last the 5-10 minutes my Race for the Galaxy games use to last.
-> The dice are very small (yes, yes, I know there are too many of them and it would have affected its price considerably).


Conclusion: The best dice conversion ever (not even close!) and a great game in itself, with an incredibly short lenght.

First impression [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/184429/item/3912544#item3912544]here[/url].

RATING: 8
Race for the Galaxy veterans are right at home here. Not too hard for new players either because both the tile effects and the turn sequence are very simple. Planets are especially easy to grasp since they have no permanent effects at all, and their instant effect can be very quickly seen from the icon alone. The only tricky part is the million different types of Reassign powers on Developments;  these are not obvious without reading the tile text.

I really like how payments for develop/settle can be accumulated and stacked, which also lowers the luck factor of rolls. Other than that, the game plays pretty much like you'd expect based on Race. Of course, all the rolling behind screens means that this can be played only with absolutely reliable players.

Lots of recycled artwork but that does not diminish the experience. Cardboard quality is somewhere between Chinese and German, i.e. does not stink but has somewhat bland colors and the cutting is rougher than in German games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Great game, lots of replayability. A little complicated in teaching others but it's worth it for a nice relaxed experience with great components.

RATING: 8
maybe the best dice game I've played.  First: it does away with the Yahtzee! mechanic (re roll dice you don't like).  Second: it mitigates bad luck with an automatic reassign (i.e. One die gets flipped to whatever face you wish) and with the "Dictate" mechanic (give up one die to reassign a second, once per turn).  Third: once used, dice must be bought back, making for some important economic choices (I.e., not everything you do can do to building your economic engine).

How does it compare to Race?  I like it way better!  One, it cuts out most of Race's symbology which has made Race a no-go for anyone I've played it with who wasn't a prior CCG/LCG player. Two, fewer tiles (analogous to cards in Race) means fewer power combos to give deep players huge advantages. Three: totally anecdotal, but it feels like I can actually accomplish more of what I set out to do in a turn than in Race. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
GH

RATING: 8.5
As a fan of Race for the Galaxy and dice games, I just had to try the dice version. Turns out it's even better than the card game (which I still consider the best in its own category).

The dice randomness is entirely mitigated by the rules and the set of tiles you can take advantage of. You can always do something each round, and with a little planning even bad rolls can be used to your advantage.

Every game feels different, and the wide choice of starting tiles really helps with that.

Oh, and the art doesn't bother me like it does with Race. Meaning it's not so bad, in this new game.

j. ANG
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[b]Roll For the Galaxy[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (10):[/COLOR]

[i]Race for the Galaxy is my favorite game and I love dice games so I couldn't wait for this to be released. It definitely exceeded my expectations, Roll manages to capture the essence of what makes Race great, but delivers it in a very different play experience. The expansion adds a lot to the base game without introducing new mechanics. The added starting tiles offer the opportunity to draft your starting tiles similar to Race and the new dice build on what was introduced in the base game by allowing you additional flexibility with dice placement. I will mention that Race is still my #1 game, but Roll is probably #3 so not too shabby.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (10):[/COLOR]

[i]- Excellent translation of Race for the Galaxy into a meaty dice game.
- Beautiful art and production quality, everything feels/looks top notch.
- Much easier to teach than Race; the iconography is greatly reduced and the tiles have their powers printed on them.
- Double sided tiles are a great solution for the explore action, there are plenty of times when you really would benefit from both options.
- Having produce before ship is more intuitive and helps streamline the produce/ship strategy and
- Purple die; boosts the produce/ship strategy by acting as a wild color (effectively increasing the value of goods consumed).
- There are a lot of strategies to explore in this game giving Roll high replay value.[/i]

RATING: 8
規則:
*每一個人就多12VP
*起始一塊錢(錢上限是10)，隨機種族版塊跟母星版塊，隨機抽兩塊星球，一個放在發展區，一個星球區，三顆白骰放骰杯，兩顆放住民區，根據起始種族跟星球拿對應骰放在住民區，骰杯或是貨物區
*遊戲進行到分數被拿完或是有人建到第12個星球，該輪結束 分數高的勝利
*每輪執行順序
1.擲骰杯內的骰
2.分配骰子 > 先照骰面放在對應位置 (*是百搭可以隨便放) > 選一顆骰不管骰面放在這回合想要的行動上 > 有重放能力可以移動下面的骰 (每個玩家可以移動一顆骰到右邊，等下放回骰杯，來移動一顆其它骰)
3.打開 > 根據玩家有選擇的行動將對應行動版塊翻正(兩人遊戲在多骰一顆來開啟一個行動) > 沒有被選的行動骰丟回骰杯
4.行動 > 照順序執行行動，一顆骰子代表一次行動
A:探索 > 拿版塊(可先隨意棄版塊，在抽棄版塊數+1的版塊，選好放在現有的版塊下面)或是得兩塊錢
B:發展 > 完成發展版塊，骰子數目等於左上角數字，一回合可以多塊，沒得用的回到骰杯
C:開拓 > 完成星球版塊，規則同發展，星球都有一次獎勵 
D:生產 > 選一個非灰色星球產貨，星球存貨上限是1
E:消費 > 拿貨換錢(根據星球顏色)或換分(基本1分，如果貨物顏色跟星球顏色一樣+1分，如果運貨骰跟星球顏色一樣在+1分，紫色百搭永遠算一樣)
使用完的工人放在住民區，沒辦法用的丟回骰杯
5.管理 > 花錢從居民區放骰到骰杯擲到錢沒或居民沒，錢如果歸零會自動+1，可以把發展開拓跟貨物取的骰放回骰杯，重置行動版塊
*最後總分為分數token加上星球左上角數字(包含起始種族版塊)，6+的發展額外加分(小數無條件進位)
*平手比全部骰杯內骰子加錢數
*多數平行處理，如果需要順位則由起始種族版塊數字最小的開始順時針進行
*蓋著跟丟棄的版塊都是秘密資訊(蓋著的被問只需公佈花費)

Basically, the effects are much less interesting and the constant dice manipulation decisions grow wearying rather than more exciting as the game progresses.

RATING: N/A
I'm kind of disappointed with this game. I've been enjoying my plays and still hasn't given up on it, but I'm kind of surprised that all the rave reviews failed to mention some pretty major faults, in my opinion:
First, there is almost no player interaction. This is the definitely the most mutliplayer solitare in my collection, and I don't know it will stay that way. Sure, the phase selection is pretty big but by golly, that's all there is. If someone gets a great engine going early on because of their faction and a good set up planet, there's literally nothing you can do about it. If you're digging for something and you're not finding it, that's basically it for you. The only other way you can affect another player is going for victory point and speeding up/slowing down the end game condition. Sure, skilled players would pay attention to what other people are doing to try and guess what phases they're going to trigger - but that's a lot of work for very little payoff. Your guess is usually not that much better than assuming equal probabilities. And it's really easy to just focus on what you're doing, and not care too much about what's happening elsewhere. The simultaneous play, while it is assume as there's really no downtime in the game, only exacerbates the problem. Half of the time you won't even see what other people are doing.
Second, there is basically no theme. Which is shocking, as SU&SD praised it for the match of theme and mechanics - I think it's the posterchild of the absence of such connection. I mean, it could be just anything - it's exactly like Spyrium in this way, you have techniques that give you long term abilities and mines that give you stuff. It could have been coal, spyrium, gold, you name it - you could place it anywhere. The pretty dice are just... dice. The blue is blue, the green is green - you never feel like you're splicing genes when you're producing green, you're just producing green. Red has absolutely nothing to do with military, the names are just empty. Perhaps it's because lots of people played race for the galaxy beforehand and I haven't, but the game feels nothing like doing anything in space. It's just an engine building game with production facilities that rewards efficiency. A good one, but it has no theme. I haven't even looked at the art of my tiles or read their titles until I finished my first game - I never really had to think about it while playing, and I think that's a big failure of a game if it doesn't evoke its own theme.

That being said, it's one of the more satisfying engine building games that I've played. The dice rolling and luck mitigation is interesting and exciting. Choosing a phase and guessing what other phases might come is really fun and the risk/reward involved is awesome. The components are great, and I love everything about the simultaneous placement of the dice. For my money, executing the phases should be done sequentially so that people will see what other people are doing. It doesn't take that long anyway (maybe except for exploring, but that's also most important). You just wait your turn to develop/settle/ship, it takes a minute.

In sum, I will play it a few more times with more players but I won't play it with my game group. It's a good game for new people, or people who love avoid conflicts, or people who play slowly - it's a nice little puzzle with a sliver of interaction when you try to figure out what phases other players will go for.

RATING: 5

I will continue to play Race hundreds of times per year (mostly online), but I doubt I play Roll five more times total.

Billed as the "dice version of Race for the Galaxy", Roll for the Galaxy doesn't attempt to present a game that's easier or less deep.  As in Race, this game is at its core a role selection game that's about resource management, but instead of the cards being resources, the dice are.  While this game doesn't present the multitude of confusing icons, the array of choices is still dizzying--exemplified by the daunting wall of text on your player screen.  This one definitely take a half-dozen plays just to understand how the system works.  Not sure I'm up for that kind of investment.

RATING: 8
So.. the dice version of my all time favourite (and most played) board game/card game - Race for the Galaxy. What surprised me most was that the play time for this game is actually longer than the the game it´s based on. But this is a good thing - this allowed this game to have it´s own feet and have almost the depth the card game has. It certailny has been simplified in some areas, but then again the action selection system is much more compicated and deep. The dice luck is well mitigated with the freedom of choosing one die to do any phase you want and also thanks to various powers to manipulate dice. Components are very pretty (although engraved dice would have been nice) and artwork is just amazing. Some of the art has been reused from RftG, but not too much. Of course I still like RftG itself better (more elegant design, plays quicker while still is deeper and more complex) but currently Roll is the hotter game for me - lot to discover for me still.

RATING: 8
I really wasn’t sure what to expect from this. I’m a big fan of Race, and dice re-implementations often fall short of their progenitors. However, the designers of Roll have done something very clever. Instead of dumbing the game down by making it a press-your-luck experience, Roll comes in as a tight, thinky dice game more akin to games like Castles of Burgundy or Troyes than Roll Through the Ages and its ilk. Players experienced with Race will jump right into Roll with just a quick explanation. I’ve heard Roll lauded as easier to teach than Race, but I’m not sure that’s entirely the case. Sure, all development and world abilities are explained via text, but there are still plenty of unfamiliar icons that players will have to grasp. 

Regardless, Roll is a really satisfying experience that begs for repeated plays, even back-to-back (a rarity in my groups). I’m not sure it has the same depth as Race. Exploration is more difficult, so the game seems a bit more tactical in that players will often be making the most of the tiles they draw rather than digging for tiles specific to a preferred strategy. Perhaps surprisingly, I don’t feel that the dice are too lucky as the game provides ways to mitigate bad rolls. 

This game sings on so many levels - some depth, interesting tactical play, engine/combo-building, variable set-up, and all in a short play time. For now it’s an 8/10, with a chance to go up.

RATING: N/A
This is a phenomenally fun, laid back competitive economic game wrapped up in a clever sci fi theme.  Each player can "name" one of five game phases per turn, and only phases named by a player actually happen - but you can "piggy back" your workers on phases your opponents named, leading to a fun and intuitive guessing game where you try to predict your opponent's likely declaration.  This can lead to hilarious accidents with two players each saying "AARGH, I thought YOU were going to name X phase!", having second-guessed each other simultaneously...

Plus you get to shake a big batch of pretty dice in a cup.  Noisy fun!  :)

RATING: 9
Having played Race for the Galaxy many times, I like Roll for the Galaxy better. Not just because I can actually win at it against my local group, but some of the mechanics help support a more balanced game I find. I feel the changes to Consume (now called Ship) make for a more balanced game, reducing the problem from Race where you can have many producing worlds with no ability to consume what you produce (thus requiring you to call Consume: Trade to extract any value from them).

It can take a few plays to learn how to read the game state (which might make veteran Race players feel uncomfortable because they're back in a more "novice" position). Overall though, the dice mechanic is well-rounded, a bit chaotic, but overall an excellent and novel way to explore the game.

RATING: 7
I was never super into Race for the Galaxy, but I liked it. This game for me blows Race out of the water. It's got a similar feel but it uses this mechanism where each player simultaneously selects their main action in secret, then reveals, and if you guessed what other players were planning, you get rewarded by following their action. I really find that so much fun. Plus the dice are cute like little dicemaster dice. I love that the buildings are verbose and explain what they do to you plainly. I wish there were more ways to really effect the other players. It seems like once a player gets a lead it's hard to overtake them. Nearly every game we have a runaway leader with no way to effect them.

RATING: 7.5
A very fun engine-building optimization game, with a good mix of luck and strategy. It's moderately easy to learn and get into, but there is definitely more to think about as you get more comfortable with the game. I like all the different ways you can modify and reassign your dice, so you don't feel too screwed over by a bad roll. I also like that you have to pay attention to what everyone else is doing, both this turn and their overall strategy, so you know what phases are likely to be activated this turn and how long you might have until the game ends. It also plays pretty quickly and leaves me with that "one more game" feeling.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Have you ever played Race for the Galaxy? It's a game where you draw cards, play cards, buy cards, and activate cards, all by spending and using cards you have. AND you choose two actions each turn to activate, and you get to use the actions that other people activate too. It's a game that has a huge following, and lots of people love it. 

I THINK THAT IT IS TRASH. Race for the Galaxy (RftG) is a game of pseudoengine building, wherein you need to have the luck to draw good cards, and then get them in the combinations you need. Optimization of how you use them really doesn't exist; you either get the cards you need or you don't. Nothing about it clicks with me, and it's awful.

Roll for the Galaxy (RollftG) fixes ALL OF THIS. The cards that you use for everything in RftG and are also planets and tech are now just Tiles, and are only used as the things they actually represent. Currency, actions, and the like are now represented by a dice pool that you spend, rehire, and reuse, and generates your available actions in a manipulable fashion, as you acquire, use, and rehire the dice of the colors (and corresponding face distributions) of your choice. This game has actual engines, a buildup represented both in planets and tech built, as well as a tangibly growing die pool, and an ability to buy points or cash (for rehiring spent dice), and to just make an effective engine.

HUZZAH FOR RollftG, and may RftG rot in the hell of ever descending pointy knives.

RATING: 6.5
Oct 2016: Played again. Surprisingly fast! However, I still have the same problem with this one as I have with Race for the Galaxy; the actions that I'm doing just don't make sense to me! I don't feel like I'm settling, developing, producing, or shipping. I feel like I'm moving dice around.

Feb 2015: I had a surprisingly amount of fun with this one compared to Race for the Galaxy!

* Although it takes way more space than RftG, I found it a bit less fiddly. There's an additional resource, dice, that's easier to manipulate and visualize than face-down playing cards

* Not being able to perform an action you haven't specifically chosen is more dramatic -- in this game, you're able to perform an action more than once (depending on the dice you roll)

Things that slow the game down or make it clunkier are the use of tiles over cards and the screen that hide the dice. The game really slows down with the Exploration phase as players have to (a) wait for the tile sack, (b) decide how many tiles to get rid of (if any) and (c) decide which side of the tile to use.

RATING: 9
Things I like about Roll for the galaxy:

1) I enjoy how the luck of die rolling is mitigated by the way you place the die.  You can always place one die on any phase regardless of result, which is great.

2) It does a great job of being different from Race for the Galaxy, while still feeling like Race.  All of us that played felt it helped a lot to be familiar with the original game, but that Roll for the galaxy was very much it's own game.

3) The components are well made.  The tiles are thick, durable, and pleasant to look at.  The bags are nice and sturdy.  The dice, while pretty much "no frills" in the looks department, are very functional and easy to read.  Considering how many dice are in the game, I am glad they kept cost down and went with the "spartan" die appearance.

Things I don't like:

Currently I have no issues with Roll for the Galaxy.  Repeated plays may change this, but for now I am quite pleased.

RATING: 8
Excellent dice rolling game with worker placement, simultaneous action selection, and just enough dice manipulation to mitigate random die rolls while still maintaining uncertainty of the outcome.

We played a 2 player game and it works excellently without resorting to a full dummy player.  I can't wait to try it with more players, as it seems as though it would scale well.

The theme seems pretty pasted on, as I barely paid any attention to the names of the tiles I was building.  I mainly only cared about their game effects.  However, the mechanics are engaging enough that this didn't bother me.  Theses mechanics have created a deep game that seems to have many different strategies that I can't wait to explore.

There can be some analysis paralysis when deciding what to dice to use, however we found this to be fairly uncommon and only lasted 10-ish seconds when it did occur.  The dice face probability table on the screen is an incredible resource to help situations like this.

RATING: 8.2
RFTG is one of my favourite games but difficult to teach and get others to play.  Like an onion, the layers of strategy only reveal themselves after many plays.  This along with the iconography makes it fall flat for new players.

Roll promises more of the same, but with an easier way to teach and text to explain each icon.

Race is a really good 2 player game , giving each player 2 actions per turn.  Roll doesn't do this so and the player count of 3 or more seems to be the sweet spot.

They both have that mechanism of leaching extra actions off other players, if, you can guess what action they would take it.  So, I would disagree with comments about it being a solitaire game.

Overall, i've not played Roll enough to feel i can fully appreciate & compare to Race but the people that have tried it with me, have all enjoyed it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I've heard a lot of hype about this game in my gaming group, yet strangely managed to never play it or even see it played. So many people I know declared how great they found this game, yet it never seemed to get brought along to gaming meetups. With such vocal backing, it was inevitable that we'd pick it up at some point - which is just what we did a while back.

To start with, the look and components. I have to say, I do like the box art. It's well done and functional, but not flashy. The box is sturdy and holds all components comfortably, with room for expansions (something I really like in a game!). The game tiles are all thick and sturdy, and a good play size, with nice art on both sides. Given how the dice are used ingame, their size is small but appropriate. The quality of the dice is quite good, with all sides identifiable as to their action. The secrecy/information screen feels a little more flimsy, compared to other components, and isn't really a great size/shape if really trying to hide your dice from others. Of course, a simple solution to this is to play with friends who won't cheat - so I don't think this is a major issue.

As a colour blind gamer, I do take issue with the colours of the dice/cards. So many of the different dice just look the same to me, that it really takes away from being able to play the game seamlessly. I am aware that the game was specifically tested with a R/G colour blind tester - and the designers implemented all of his suggestions! Unfortunately, the print run didn't come back like the prototypes. So it's hard to hold the colour issue against them. Since my girlfriend has promised to mark all the dice/cards for me - I can't give negative marks here :) 

Moving onto the gameplay, and I admit that there is a bit to understand in the rules for the game. However, once these are understood, then a game round can move quite quickly. Each dice action is pretty easy to work with once you know what each one does. The game does get a bit more complex when you have multiple developments, and you have to keep track of abilities yourself (or watch other players, if you don't trust them....). But, all in all, a game round is pretty simple and moves quickly.

I read a lot of complaints about the dice in the gameplay. Being a dice based game, dice will go for and against you over the course of a game. That's the nature of rolling a six sided dice. The gameplay is completely based about getting your dice to do actions. Whichever player gets the most dice consistently active each turn, they will win. "Lucky" rolls can't be depended on, ever. But we do have the ability to manipulate our dice! 
We start with two ways to manipulate your rolled dice (choosing your action, and the dictator ability), but can obtain more by building developments. When you have dice manipulation effects in games, getting more is generally the only choice. If you do it, then you gain consistency in your turns - so you can actually choose a strategy to pursue. If you don't - then you're just playing with random dice luck. Either it'll go well and you'll feel great - or it'll go bad and you'll curse the game for just being bad luck. Given that this game is completely about action economy - the more of your dice that are being used for actions, each turn, the better. Getting dice manipulation abilities in other games normally just comes with a cost, given how strong they make the rest of your game. Here, the developments that give you dice manipulation effects ALSO give you points! Um, hell yeah! Now the race is to get these effects early, so all my subsequent turns are more consistent. This means now we have an extra luck element ingame - do you get lucky and draw the cards early that give you dice manipulation or do you not? Add to that, the starting planet/abilities - some are just much stronger than others. Another "getting lucky" draw at the start of the game to add. 

The theme of the game does start to get lost a bit in the gameplay. At no point do I really feel that I'm building a Space Empire. I take a planet, I get a new dice - then mostly ignore that planet (though that's partly to do with the colour blind, mixing up the colours). I get a new development, I try to remember to use my new ability. Of course, we do put the card down and our space on the table expands - but I never really got the feeling of actually building out an Empire. I guess I might just be nitpicking here.

All of these luck elements are fine, though. I don't mind dice, dominant strategies, startup luck, game theme. The next gameplay part is the bit that turns me off most about the game. The game is essentially just a single player game. Another review described it best as "Multiplayer Solitaire". For the first few turns, I took an interest in what my opponent did. How she built up, what dice she was getting, what her new abilities were. After a while, I realised that it didn't matter what she did. She couldn't affect my "Empire" at all. There is no direct interactions between players. The only way that another player affects you is in the action they choose to perform. I appreciate that you can say that there is a strategy here. If you can predict what your opponent will do, then you'll possibily get to do extra actions from dice that wouldn't have did anything that turn. My solution, in each game we've played, is to focus on getting dice manipulation effects. That way, I could properly ignore having to predict what she'd do and still have most of my dice in play each turn. This lack of player interaction is just ridiculous in a 2-5 player game.

Game turns can be pretty quick, but this is not a "fast" game. So, the lack of player interaction feeds into an issue with the lack of any catchup mechanic. If someone falls behind with bad luck early, they're really just playing with no way to ever catch the leader. If there was some kind of player interaction, then the bottom players could collectively gang up to slow down the leader - and give themselves a chance to catchup. But no, the leader is playing his own single player map - so some players will be out of the game, but still forced to play to the end. While our games so far have not seen a runaway leader, I can easily see it happen in 4/5 player games. 


My feelings, as you might guess, are a little torn. So, if I ask myself:
"Is there a game here?" - Yes, there is. There is fun in building up of your "Empire" and it all looks nice with decent quality components. 
"Will I play it again?" - Again, yes. I think I know too many people who seem to really like it to avoid it. 
"Is this a game that I'd ever suggest playing?" - I'd have to say, no. If I'm playing a game with other people, I want to play "with" or "against" them. But here, I'm really doing neither until counting up the scores at the end. There are too many better games on our shelves for me to ever suggest taking this one down.

Going by the BGG rating system, for me it slots in easily as: "4 - Not so good - but could play again."
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

While I like dice games, I worry that the luck may be a little high in this one, especially in the 2 player version.  I also worry that the starting worlds are not balanced -- better quality shipping worlds seem vastly better for bootstrapping than a few extra points.  Both issues seem to stem from the importance of shipping to keep your engine running.  Thus, starting with shipping infrastructure and rolling the right faces to keep it going are both important.

update: after another play, I found the game clean but frustrating.  If you don't draw cheap worlds early, you can't get more dice to bootstrap.  If you don't roll settle icons, you can only gain new dice inefficiently.  Once things get rolling, it's fine, but the early game is random and influential.

None-the-less, I find the game somewhat pleasing.

decent enough game, but i think there are only two reasons this remains in my collection: 1.) solos fairly well, and 2.) i don't already own Race. also, the wife likes it, although neither of us care to play often. it's awfully swingy and random, although, i have to admit, not as chaotic as you'd expect a dice game to be. when i first learned the game it was from guys who could seriously compete and expect to win most games. good for them. my wife and i don't care to memorize a few "best tiles" and dig through the bag to find them. actually, i think we don't dig through the bag nearly as much as we probably should. okay, but that's not really the game we'd like to be playing and i have others which really do this for us. hmm, maybe i will get rid of this game. i'm still up in the air whether i'll buy Race or just stick with Jump Drive. i'd recommend this game to anyone with a small collection who wanted to buy a single engine building game which rewards repeated plays, limits boring downtime (simultaneous turns!), and definitely ends when someone is winning (can only build 12 tiles, no switching out early bad tiles for better ones if you're suddenly rich). not recommended for kids since it's not at all hard to cheat and the vigorous cup rattling could easily become too intense for aunts and uncles.

RATING: 7
16-06-2015. Sold after 4 plays. I do like the game, but its hard for me to get to the table. I am not interested enough in the game to keep it around to explore it further. Somehow, these kind of games never hit the table.

07-05-2015. Rated after 3 plays. Err.. I didn't like this so much with 2 player. Engine ground to a halt, and many of the dice each turn were wasted utterly.

26-04-2015. Rated after 1 play. I can teach Roll for the Galaxy I think.

It's easy. Each turn, everyone rolls the dice they have in their cups. With the rolled dice, you can activate a role, and take actions showing the die-faces. You are only sure that your role will activate and any action you assigned with it. If you assign dice to a role you don't take yourself, you have to hope/think other players will take the roll.

Using this system, you explore planets and technologies to develop. You research or explore those, both represented by one role. Then one role lets you charge planets with trade goods an lastly you can ship those trade goods to get money or VPs. At the end of your turn, you spend money to regain dice you rolled. If at any time you are not able to take an action because the role was not chosen, you also get those dice back free.

Game is elegant, easy and fun to play. It's quite light, but I suspect interesting depth here. So far, I am intriguied. I do wonder a little if the game has legs, or if it's better than Race. I have not played race.. so I am curious to try that. For now, Roll is on my wish-list, fi it ever come across a cheap-ish copy, I'll instabuy.

RATING: 4
Race for the Galaxy is one of only three games that I give a [bgcolor=#00CC00][s][color=#00CC00]-[/color][/s][b]10[/b][s][color=#00CC00]-[/color][/s][/bgcolor] rating to. Roll for the Galaxy is essentially an abomination. Alright, not quite an abomination on the level of Ra: The Dice Game. Let's be kinder and say it is instead "a blasphemy." Happier now?

I look forward to watching with bemusement as the critical reception of this game plays out the arc that Trains (a Dominion clone) took a couple years ago.

Phase 1--where we are at as of the moment I'm typing this--is The Gushing, and the "Finally This Replaces..." etc. blah blah. This kind of nonsense will come from variously-motivated folk.

Some of it will come from people who genuinely love Race, and since this game has such a strong echo of Race, these fans will find it to be a very pleasant lark. That's great.

But honestly, I think a lot of The Gushing will come from sincere casual gamers who--though they wanted to like Race--had trouble enjoying it. This would be the crowd you sometimes hear Deploring the Icons. (Which is such nonsense! But that's another tangent!) More likely? Perhaps because they probably had the misfortune of playing Race with other gamers who PLAY A WHOLE LOT OF RACE. And kind of like people who try Dominion casually, when you go up against someone in one of these card games--an opponent who has played the game a thousand times, seriously a thousand times--you are not likely to have a wonderful experience of it. So here's a lot of people who might have been predisposed to like Race, and now are getting a second life on that. Which is great, really.

But as Phase 1 winds down, things will begin to get a little quieter. Roll for the Galaxy is, after all, not a great game. So as that becomes apparent, those that love Race will return to Race, and those that gave Roll a try will drift on to the next thing, except...

Phase 2 (a.k.a. "The Rising Sun Phase"): Suddenly, enthusiasm will get re-lit, as we learn more and more of an impending expansion. The Gushers from Phase 1--who had become strangely quiet--will now reappear briefly. Previous Gushings will have disappeared down the Memory Hole, but the New Line will be that this long-awaited expansion will--what are the phrases we use?--Solve Some of the Problems, or better yet, Add Some Needed Variation to what was, after all, a Good Game that Wasn't Quite There Yet. This phase leads naturally into...

Phase 3. It's a couple of years later. Nobody is talking about or playing the game. Casual players will have drifted off to the next thing, because, well, because that's what casual players do. When cornered, die-hard Race fans will admit that, while Roll for the Galaxy was a lark for a while, in the end, they returned to Race because "it is A Bit More Fun, is A Bit Less Random, and has A Bit More Depth."

Which of course is French for: "it was an abomination."

Err...check that. "A blasphemy."  ;)

RATING: 9
Similar theme, different mechanics! Definitely not just a dice *version* of the card game!

I found Roll for the Galaxy mechanically different from Race ("If you've played Race for the Galaxy, you will need to unlearn some rules. If you haven't played Race for the Galaxy, you'll have to learn the rules. Either way, you're even."), and introduce the game this way to experienced gamers. It's kind of information and strategy dense, and most of the players I played with already played Race for the Galaxy. Feel free to post how you teach the game.

"Here's the engine: You need Galactic Credits to move dice from the Citizenry (aka. the unemployment line) to the Cup, use the dice from the Cup to assign to Phases, then return dice used in Phases to the Citizenry. You place Worlds to gain more dice to roll. Developments give you additional abilities. Colored dice are better than the white Home dice.

"Right now, most of you have only one Galactic Credit. You need Galactic Credits to put dice into the Cup from the Citizenry. You can either use Explore to get more Galactic Credits, or Trade, if you have a Good on one or more of your Worlds.

"Roll is a classic "The dice hate you" game. You will either have one Plan A, or multiple Plan C's. There is no Plan B. You can only select one Phase, but may want or be able to do something during other phases. So take a look at other player's worlds. If they have no Goods, they may Produce. If they have a Good, they may Ship. Grey Worlds produce no Goods."

We play the tutorial game open-handed. We analyze and discuss each player's desired moves in detail, and introduce the rules necessary for the player to accomplish this. Answer questions, but focus on questions related to what the current player wants to do. The rules for Exploring to gain Galactic Credits is easy, so we start with a player who's mostly likely to do this, typically a player with no Goods and at least one die under Explore. We first calculate how many GC's he would need to move all his dice into his Cup next turn, then determine how many dice he needs in his Explore phase, then how to select this phase, then Reassignment, if necessary. Next, if a player has a Good on a world, we discuss Trade. If a player has a cheap Development or World in his Construction area, we discuss the Development and Settle phases.

If not all the rules have been introduced, we continue to the next turn. The tutorial ends when each phase has been covered (two turns for five players), making sure all the important details, like the Purple die in scoring VP's, have been mentioned. Not surprisingly, this tutorial takes a long time (two hours for five players) -- but players now know both the rules, and how to plan their moves!

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This was my favorite game from AGF #25. It's a "dice variant" of Race for the Galaxy, a card game that I rarely play because it shares mechanics with San Juan which I think do them better. But Roll for the Galaxy is a novel take on the subject. Familiarly, you need to complete 12 tiles to trigger the game end, the cost to build is a tile is also its victory points, and there are variable phases. Beyond that, it's a fast and interesting dice-allocation game with a huge pile of dice. It's almost solitaire, but there's a subtle depth to the one piece of interaction, which is choosing which one phase you will secretly lock-in. Since only locked phases occur, players take a keen interest in what the others are doing, leading to some gambling and bluffing. I enjoyed it quite a bit!

RATING: 8
I think Race for the Galaxy is probably a better strategy game (it's certainly faster in my experience), but I'd much rather teach Roll for the Galaxy than Race for the Galaxy. In Roll, there is a ton of variability, particularly once you add the expansion into the mix, and there is still a good amount of strategy. Occasionally one player will get just the right mix of tiles and steamroll everyone else, but most of the time there is a fair amount you can do to improve your chances. Roll is one of my favorite dice games. 

That said, development and settle strategies are a little too strong due to a few tiles that enable those strategies to get going a little too quickly if you get a good combination of them early. I also think a few tiles that enable develop or settle strategies are too efficient. Consume strategies are more viable in higher player count games, where you increase the chance of multiple players calling produce/ship, but they only work in low player count games when no one gets a strong combination for building.
