RATING: 2
(after 1 play)

Nope. Not as good. 

Firstly I must say it's an impressive re-imagining of RFTG, a testament to the designer skills of those involved. The result is actually better than many dice versions of games are, maybe even on the higher level than the line of FFG's another re-imaginings or Arkham Horror. It just fails to deliver exactly the thing I play RftG for, instead this is replaced by the kind of things that make me run away from many euro games.  

RollFTG is a bit simpler than RFTG, way more accessible, because it is in essence an engine building game. I wanted to say I like RFTG because it forces me to think on the spot while RollFTG doesn't, but actually both allow me to think on the spot. However: RFTG demands of me strategic, long term decisions to be made on a spot, big risks, high possible rewards. Whereas RollFTG seems mostly tactical - fiddle with this, change that, adjust this. Selection of tiles is much more random than in RFTG (because: draw engines) - which again makes the game more tactical. 

Summary: it's safer. 
It's more accessible, you don't need as much skill, learning curve is lower, there's less risk, less rewards, less interaction (not that there was much of it to start with). It's more controllable, moves slower, is more forgiving. And supposedly gives you more "choices" (by removing all the hard ones the rest became equal and suddenly more of them are viable). So: exactly what euromasses ordered.

RollFTG is a more typical and "pure" engine building game, which also makes it stand out less between all the engine building euros compared to RFTG.

EDIT rating lowered because I'm not interested in what the game offers. Solid one, but I have RFTG.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I have never played Race for the Galaxy before, but this is a fun little tableau builder in its own right. The management of dice is pretty neat and goes beyond the basic allocation of dice rolls, as you are always looking for ways to expand your dice pool and unlock dice from your citizenry. 

While there's not a ton of interaction, players have to keep an eye out for the phases which are likely to be activated by opponents. It's especially painful if you're the only player who's out of tempo with the rest of the table in producing and shipping.

The main drawback is that it's easy for players to get impatient and begin resolving all phases on their own, which leads to a mess of checking whether everyone is done with their turn. I'd avoid playing this as a 5P game again for that reason alone. Like most tableau builders, I find it's easy to overplay the game to the point of burning out. It's a great game to keep in rotation though.

RATING: 7.75
Roll for the Galaxy is a solid game where players assign rolled dice to perform actions. Players develop technologies, settle planets, and trade their way to victory points. The component quality is great while the gameplay is only good. 

Fans of Race for the Galaxy should note that with the ability to reassign dice and the double-sided nature of the development/settle tiles, there are more options and decisions in Roll than in Race, and for that I feel that Roll is the better game. Also, the iconography in Roll has accompanying text so that one doesn't have to learn a foreign language of icons just to play the game. 

I still feel that, as far as a space-themed game, Roll for the Galaxy does fall short of Eminent Domain, but that it is still a fun game to play.

RATING: 7
[2015-07-25]
Nagy elvárásaim voltak a játékkal szemben. Egyrészt a nagy előd kártyás változat magasra tette a lécet, másrészt kockás játék sokat kell, hogy mutasson, ahhoz hogy nálam megdöntse az eddigi kedvenceket. Nos 4 db 2 személyes játék után azt kell mondanom erős riválist ismertem meg a Roll for the Galaxy személyében. Kezdjük a jó tulajdonságokkal, mert abból nagyon sokat tudok sorolni. A játék egyszerű, egyszerűbb, mint a kártyás, könnyebben tanítható, gyorsan elsajátítható. Az ikonozást könnyű megtanulni. Az egyes lapkák mindig egy dolgot tudnak és azok a legtöbbször nem különlegesek. A termelő világok megoldása is egyszerűbb a kártyás változathoz képest, az áruk feldolgozása szintén. A kockák helyes használatát kell megérteni és ennyi. A játék gyors, ketten 30 perc vagy még annál is rövidebb idő alatt letolható egy menet és érzésem szerint öten se nyúlna 1 óra fölé, így sok parti lejátszható egymás után. Illetve érzésem szerint minden játékosszámmal jó. Sőt, úgy gondolom többen még jobb, mint ketten. A játék változatos, sok stratégia működik, szinte minden játékom teljesen más volt eddig. A játék szép, bár a kockák csak festettek, nem vésettek, azért szerintem elég tartósak lehetnek. A kockák természetesen visznek szerencsét bele, de mégis úgy érzem a 4 játék alatt, ha 2-3 olyan dobásom volt, amit igazán rossznak értékeltem, a többivel viszont mindig tudtam valami jót kezdeni. Ötletes a pénz szerepének megoldása. Fel kellett ugyanis váltani a lapeldobás mechanizmusát és ez kiválóra sikerült.

Jöjjön pár negatívum. Ha már a pénzzel fejeztem be, itt azzal is kezdem. Az új játékosok figyelmét érdekes módon nem hívja föl a szabály arra, hogy a pénz nagyon fontos a játékban, mert ha nem ügyelünk rá, akkor van esély, hogy több körig csak szenvedünk, míg valahogy újra nem teremtünk belőle többet mint egy. Ugyanis minden körben kapunk egyet, de ez csak egy kockával való dobást tesz lehetővé, ami biztos kudarc. Szóval elfért volna néhány tipp a szabály végén a kezdők számára. A játék nekem érzésre nem más, mint az előd. Azaz nem érezném úgy, hogy kell mindkettő, ha az egyik megvan. Bár a játék változatos, mégis egy full pasziánsz, így lehet sok játék után unalmassá válik. Pedig rengeteg lehetőség lett volna az interakció megteremtésére. Elég ha csak arra gondolok, hogy a saját fejlesztés táblák helyett közös lett volna. Szóval érdekes, hogy a nagy előd legnagyobb kritikáját, a pasziánsz jellegét ebben sem orvosolták. Mivel én szeretem az ilyet, engem nem zavar, de biztos sokakat igen. A téma nekem még annyira sem jön át, mint a kártyás változatban. Még annyit sem kell nézni a lapkákat, mint a testvérnél.

Összességében tetszett a játék, a jó kockás társasok között a helye. Kíváncsi vagyok, hogy jól érzem-e, hogy többen még jobb lehet, ha sikerül kipróbálni, akkor mondok rá végső ítéletet.

[2017-11-05]
Igaz, csak eggyel többen, de hárman játszva jobb a játék, talán 4-en még jobb lehet, mert sokkal több akció végrehajtódik, így a kockák elosztásakor megnövekvő időt a több akció kompenzálja. A szerencse azért erősebb, mint a kártyás változatban és lehet sajnos nagyon szerencsételenül is dobni. Marad a pontszám, de kb. bármikor kategória.

RATING: 8
1 play

A very cool take on the ideas in Race for the Galaxy, but now with dice. This, though, is not just a twist on Yahtzee: the dice, with no reroll (but you can spend one to reallocate) are used in a great way to select actions and to power them. It still feels like Race, but plays faster and seems easier to get into for new players.

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
Players get a random starting setup: 1 home planet, 1 development and 1 world to build, and a set of dice based on that (plus 3 white dice). Each of the colored dice have a different distribution of faces, as indicated on the player aid.

Each round, players simultaneously roll their dice behind their screens. Place all dice in groups according to their face, then use any one of them to choose one of the 5 game actions (explore, develop, settle, trade/consume, produce). Reveal all dice, then resolve the actions in order.
- Explore: for each die, draw n+1 tiles (n=the number of unbuilt develop/settle tiles you have on your board that you choose to discard; all discards returned to bag at END of this phase). Each tile is double sided, with settlement on one side and development on the other. Choose which side you want, and put tiles at the bottom of the appropriate stacks.
- Develop: move all your develop dice to top development tile (which is finished when it has x dice on it; x=number on the tile)
- Settle: like develop, but for the settlement tile
- Trade: earn $2/die, or sell goods from planets (need a die on the world, and a sell die) for $3-6 (depends on type).
- Consume: use each die, matched with good, to earn VP; base 1VP + 1VP for each die matching the color of the planet where the good is coming from (so max 3VP)
- Produce: place each produce die on a settlement world, max 1 good/world

The game ends when someone has a total of 12 built worlds/settlements, or if the VP pile (starts with 12/player) is empty. Most VP wins!

RATING: 9
This is officially my favorite dice version of another game.  I typically don't like dice versions of other games, especially games I already adore and I do adore Race for the Galaxy.  I like dice just fine but typically the way dice versions simplify mechanics of games into dice rolls is akin to surgery with a hatchet.  There are some I do enjoy though and this one was so completely well done.

This will feel very familiar if you have played Race for the Galaxy.  Each player has an income track as well as some number of developments or planets in their queue for researching or settling.  Players also have a prep area for dice as well as a dice cup.  Each round players will buy as many dice as they can/want to from their prep area into their cup, roll them, and assign them to actions.  One die each round can select any phase regardless of what side was rolled.  This guarantees that phase will be played.  The other dice have to go under the columns for the side rolled (with some exceptions such as powers from developments and planets).  So some dice may be wasted if they aren't under the phase you selected and no other player selected it either.

The phases are almost identical to normal Race.  Explore will get you new developments or planets into your queues or money.  Research assigns dice to a development, when it is fully researched it goes into your tableau and the dice are released to your prep area.  Same with settle and planets.  There are also produce and ship phases which put dice on planets as resources and sells them for money or points.  Even the end game is the same as normal Race.  12 tiles or all the VP chits are taken.

RATING: 8.5
What a great game!  Is it multiplayer solitaire?  Yes, totally.  But it is a perfect balance of acceptable luck (for a less-than 1 hour game) and mostly strategic play.  There is some tactical play, but generally your choices are based on a turn or two downstream.

Dropped by rating by a point since it's entirely possible for a player to get completely screwed by unlucky rolls and poor tile draws.  Especially in a 5 player game this can make the game drag on forever for that player as they watch everyone else race ahead.

Likewise it's even more possible that a single player will end up with incredibly synergistic tile draws that give them a lead which can never be slowed or caught.

Still a solid game that will remain in my collection for a while, just not quite as perfectly balanced as it appeared when we first played.

RATING: 6
I'm sure it trivializes the development that went into this game to say that it is exactly like you would expect of Race for the Galaxy: the Dice Game.

As in Race, the core of the game is the set of turn phases. When a player rolls their dice, one can be used to choose any phase (which will occur for all players), while the rest must go against the symbol rolled and will activate only if one or more players has chosen that phase. Different coloured dice have different symbol distributions and also score bonus points if they colour-match on "consume" actions.

Watching opponents and leeching from their actions is required for good play in Race too, but the way dice are assigned here forces even beginners to pay attention. This contributes to a general feeling that the Roll is far more accessible than its forebear, which is notoriously hard on new players and I find toes the line of acceptable complexity. (It's much better with computer assistance than without.)

But the luck of the draw seems more pronounced, not to mention the luck of the dice. Worse, I don't get the same sense of depth that made Race such an achievement in design. 

Without the misfortune of being compared to its ancestor I'd probably just call it a good-but-not-great example of a type of game I dislike, but as it is it will always remind me of a better game I would rather be playing.

RATING: 6
Rolling dice is fun.  Rolling dice in a cup is extra fun.  The game and dice are all so colorful, it looks pretty.

Regarding the mechanics, there is quite a bit less luck than you might think from just rolling dice.  Each die has a slightly different action combo on each of its faces, so you can use that to weigh initial probability.  Then there is also a mechanic where you can set aside one die, which allows you to move another die to another action other than what the die face shows.  Then there are tiles you can buy to further change those odds.

Overall, it is a very fun building game.

RATING: 10
tl;dr: similar yet different

[thing=28143][/thing] has long been one of my favorites and it is my most played game by a huge margin. [thing=21632][/thing] is a dice game that I enjoy quite a bit from the same designer. So [person=150][/person] doing a dice game based on RFTG sounded promising.

I've already played the game twice now, and I've enjoyed both games. You can definitely recognize those RFTG genes in there but it's not a straight conversion. It does bring some new and interesting mechanisms with it. To does feel quite similar yet quite different at the same time.

It seems like there's less of a 'yellow strategy' or 'rebel strategy'. I think the main reason for that is that the amount of tiles that pass through your hands is significantly lower than the amount of cards you get to see in RFTG. So there is less planning and hoping and more using what you get to the best of your abilities.
I think the strategy in this game comes more from managing your dice. Certain colors are better for doing certain actions, so getting the balance right there while still building up a steady income stream to reuse your dice is what the game is all about.

I look forward to exploring this game more because it is clear that there is more there than 2 plays will expose.

A comment on the components: while generally of good quality (don't reach the German standard though) the dice are very cheap looking. Screen printed, really? If this game sees as much play as I hope it will, that will not do, the icons will fade quickly. I expect RGG to send me a free replacement set if that ever happens. The icons should have been embossed.

Initial rating: 7.2/10
BGG scale: 7/10

RATING: 7.9
More of a worker placement game than a dice game. The dice keep gameplay interesting, but it isn't a luckfest: the distribution of symbols differs with different type of dice and there are some ways to manipulate the dice if you had a really bad roll. interesting mechanics. Every turn you get to make important decisions: which dice to take back into your cup, which phase to select (while trying to guess what your opponents will select), which type of empire to build. Goal: build the most valuable (pointwise) galactic empire faster than your opponents. RATING: 7
Roll for the Galaxy is interesting, but has a Catch 22 problem. Here it is in a nutshell. The game can be very frustrating if you don't roll what you need. Now, there are special powers you can acquire through building developments and settling planets that can change die faces, giving you lots of options. However, once you are rolling seven, eight, nine or more dice and have the ability make red dice into X, and yellow dice into Y, and blue dice into any facing you choose, things get complex and turns start to bog down. And at that point it's no longer a light dice game. Which isn't a problem if you're willing to invest more than the advertised 45 minutes into the game. Not sure at this point if I am ...

RATING: 6
Get this and then you don't need any other dice games. (Except Sagrada. Get Sagrada.)

But seriously the mountain of dice you get is awesome. I always waffle on this game though. When it's fun, it's very fun. You get your engine going and you a rolling a ton of dice and doing everything you want thanks to your engine planning. It really feels like you're doing well when you have a bunch of dice in your cup too.

However, I have some serious issues with it as well. First off, teaching it is a nightmare. The symbols are fine but I rarely pick up a tile or look at my player card and think "oh, this is what that means." Add that to the fact that it is very easy to mess up/miss a rule/accidentally cheat and it makes just playing the game exhausting. And I thought I would have fun just rolling dice!

Next, I don't find the scoring to be satisfying at all. You ship and you can get money or points. You built a tile with a high number so you get points. And that's it. May be just me but I don't feel like my goal is exciting at all.

Finally, the actual dice allocation mechanic - the key part of the game - becomes an afterthought halfway through the game. Once you get a modest engine going, you have pretty much all the options you want every turn. And even if you don't, you've amassed so many dice that you can throw away any two to make any one you want. (That rule is pretty dumb, I think.) Predicting what phases are going to happen is the most exciting part, and even that has the air let out of it a little since if you predict wrong, the dice just go back in the cup, which is not really a big deal.

In all, it has some very good bits and some bad bits.

RATING: 7.5
First Impression:
Race for the Galaxy with dice and tiles instead of cards.

What I liked:
a) Everything that applies as a good thing in Race works in Roll as well. Sense of accomplishment, ease of play, important decisions both tactical and strategic, goals, variety, push your luck, fun, and quick gameplay.
b) Components wise, Roll feels much better and much more sturdy than Race. The tiles are plain better than cards, and the fact that they are double adds some nice balance and extra choices. The dice are also great as workers.
c) The game feels less fiddly than Race somehow. Using cards as resources always felt cheap in my eyes.
d) The new economy system is much cleaner, easier to explain and gives a better output, and thus sense of accomplishment than Race.

What I didn't like:
a) Same as in Race, the interaction is too mild for my taste. 
b) It's still a snowball game. Another player pulled out a better combo? You can see you're going to lose half way in and there is nothing you can do about it, even if you play better for the rest of the game.
c) There is a bit more randomness due to the dice rolling than Race, but still it's not much of an issue as you have more capabilities, you choose your dice and you have a lot of ways to make them anything you may want.
c) The major issue is that it is extremelly easy to cheat in this game. Turning a dice to another side is impossible to catch behind a player screen. Really, don't play this unless you can trust your opponents to play fairly.

Overall it's a better game than Race IF you play it with people you trust. People that won't - at the critical moment at the last round when they just can't build that 6 cost winning development because one white dice didn't roll develop (1/6 chance) - be tempted to just roll a die to it's side behind their screens and cry victory.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Roll for the Galaxy is hard game for me to review. It obviously shares a lot with Race for the Galaxy. So the real question for me is how is it different and would I prefer one over the other.

Before I get into that though... let's talk about the graphic design of the game. There's a lot of iconography in the game, some of which is the same as Race, but they did not do a good job of making the game comprehensible. The cheat sheet in particular is undecipherable until you start understanding the game. But even given that, there has to be a better way to display the information. I feel like this is a graphic design fail.

A lot of the game is the same. There are the same actions as RftG, but although some of them are implemented slightly differently, the overall feel is much the same. The main difference is that you have dice instead of cards. And different colored dice have different distributions of actions, most leaning towards a particular set of actions.

Everybody secretly rolls up whatever dice they were able to get into their cups. Then they assign them to whatever action is displayed. They can pick a die and use it to select the action that they want to guarantee will be performed this round. And it doesn't have to be the action shown on that die... so nobody is ever screwed out of an action they really want. And they can also use a die to force another die to be something else.

Finally, everybody reveals their dice. If someone selected an action and you have dice assigned to that action, they activate. Unused dice go back into the cup, used dice have to be repurchased for a buck each. I won't go into much about how the rest of the game differs from Race, as it isn't really material to understanding the differences in the games. It's this action selection mechanic that is really the primary difference.

So how you feel about Roll vs Race comes down to how you feel about the dice. First, let me say that while it seems that this introduces a lot of randomness in the game, everybody in my first game had a way to mitigate that. So in the end, you almost always could get your dice where you wanted. And since you can use one die to select the guaranteed action, you can always have at least one die on the action you want.

What I like about the dice is that it replaces a lot of the card fatigue you feel in Race... where you're constantly shuffling, cycling through cards, exploring through a ton of cards, etc. At times it feels like they said "You know, I love shuffling... I bet I could shoehorn a game into that somehow." The dice cut the card maintenance out of the game making it a lot easier to manage.

But what I like about Race is that the game comes down to picking the right action. You want to pick an action that will help you, without helping someone else, and hopefully also plan so that other people will be picking an action you need on a turn when you don't. There's kinda that same thing in Race, but it's not nearly as pure. Someone else can profit off your actions, not because they planned to, but simply because the dice ended up on the right symbol and they didn't have a way to change them to something else. And it's this distinction that really separates the two games for me and is why I prefer the original.

I like Roll for the Galaxy... but I prefer Race for the Galaxy. I'd probably want to play Roll every once in a while for something different, but most of the time I'd probably rather play Race.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
2-5;2-5;3-4
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Want in Trade denotes silica gel protection.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[thing=132531][/thing] is, as many know, the little brother of [thing=28143][/thing]. And, as it happens in many of these case, the little brother is also much bigger, since the parents learned the tricks of raising a game and know that fooling around in dirt may actually be good for it, since it will develop better, thus being able to, being bigger, to conquer its space more easily when come the time for the little brother to leave the nest and explore the universe, taking just some goods with it in order to pay for some action. You know what I mean.

Anyway, [thing=132531][/thing] is indeed bigger: over 100 dice, cards turned into a lot of double-side tiles, players screens and boards, a dice bag, rules and plastics cups for dice rolling stravaganza and bothersome sound to the next table. No matter, I like the sound of the dice rolling and I will make some noise with it.

The game play sounds nearly the same: you choose one action and hope/foresee that the other will call some other actions you want to do. You do both by allocating dice to the actions you want to do - of course, as expected, you have to put them in the face showed after the roll, but you have some ways (and tiles) that allow you to change them from one place to the other. The dice you put in actions that weren't called, return to the cup.

The dice actually used serve basically as the cards in [thing=28143][/thing], but, again, not quite. If you put, say, three dice in the Explore action, you can use one to take a tile from the bag, another to draw other tile, and the third to gain +2 money. If you have 2 dice in the Develop action, you put both of them above the tile in the development area. If you have two dice in the Consume action, you can consume two goods, one for points and other for money, for example. The dice you put above the Develop tile and the World tile stay there, until there are enough dice to match the number required for them. Once this happen, the dice go to the citizenry area (a place in your board for the dice you have, that aren't inside your cup, being rolled).

Every round you can pay $1 for each die you want to take from your citizenry area to put it in your cup. More dice, more chances to do more actions, and also make one action A LOT. Card flow in this game became money/dice flow - you need to keep puting dice from the citizenry inside the cup, or you will may lose the timming of some important actions, and be left behind (much like being without cards in Race).

The game still ends when someone reachs twelve tiles in the personal tableau or when the vps chips end - however, now everyone starts with three tiles (one home planet, and two that represent your "race"). This might make you think: "So this game is faster, right?" No, it isn't. Between rolling the dice, allocating them, reassign them and having them locked in developments and in worlds, [thing=132531][/thing] takes longer to finish. Probably, as happenened with me, with Race, it will probably take less to finish plays with more experience, but I doubt it will ever be as fast as Race, but then, everyone knows usually running beats rolling when it comes to speed in a flat surface like a table.

[b]Ok, but it is better than Race?[/b] Well... No, for me, right now, it isn't. But I will add perspective: I played Race thousands of times, but, it took a while for me to like it at first. After my first play, I avoided it like it was the plague. I didn't understand a thing, someone had basically to play for me, and it was wasted minutes I never manage to get a refund. Once it cliked it was oh-my-god-this-is-great sort of advent. So it was a tall order for [thing=132531][/thing] to accomplish, and it failed in this matter. I found it to be fiddlier, slower and with some more luck added in (not only because of the dice, but also because of the way the tiles work - it is, indeed, awesome to have double-side tiles, so you will always get worlds and/or developments, but since you must put them in the board, the order you draw them matters a lot, as you might have to block, for the moment, something because you have to put the new tile bellow it). But this "luck" thing might just be because I know all the cards in Race, and know the ins and outs, and the same isn't true for Roll, however, I do miss the Explore+5 action, to actually look "deep" for something I truly want.

[b]And the positives?[/b] There are several. I mentioned already one of the big ones: double-sided tiles. Every tile you take have a world in one side and a development on the other. This is awesome! If you have and engine going for colonization, you know you will always find worlds (not necessarily good worlds, be in type or value, but, still, a world), and the same is valid for developments. [thing=132531][/thing] also seemed way easier to teach, mainly because you don't have anymore a Military bonus (military worlds and developments only give you red dice, which are good to colonize and develop), you don't have more windfall worlds (these worlds are still in the game, but they work as the other production worlds, except that they give a production die directly to the cup, and not the citinzery area), the Consume phase now is after the Production (makes easier to use goods), and you never have $0 in the game, so it is basically like ever having at least one card at the start of each round in Race (so Roll is more friendly). The production value is pretty good in [thing=132531][/thing]: the tiles are great, the cups are neat (and plastic doesn't smells bad like one known offender), the screens work pretty well, having some nice set of aids for the players. But I don't like the dice - I know engraving them would make the costs jump, but I don't like painted dice. I always get the feeling that the informations will peel off in no time - [thing=10640][/thing] made me like this.

[b]Will I play it?[/b] Of course. It is a good game, just not as good (so far) as [thing=28143][/thing]. I'm guessing [thing=132531][/thing] will reach a fine status alongside Race (and, of course, because being seen by some as a 2nd edition, it will get higher average ratings, which will help to keep the interest up for longer). And I like the sound of dice rolling inside a cup, so there is this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.7
It's a proper game. Not sure tho, why is it so hyped. It's a good worker placement game with some strategy in it. Also, predicting your opponent moves is crucial in this game. It can be fun, but there is almost no emotions. This game is based on a pure math, with predicting what your opponent will do. Rounds are quick, players are doing their turns at the same time. Game itself is also pretty quick, so you can play it few times in a row, with not losing so much time on it.

 Game is oriented around dices(Hence, it's called ROLL for the galaxy, duh) but it's not really random, because 6 sided dices usually have 2-3 same symbols on it, and almost all of them have a wild card symbol on it(some of them even more than one), and you can also choose a dices with symbols you preffer most. And even then, if you somehow didn't manage to get what you want, there's always an option to change your roll value to whatever you like at the cost of one dice(which you can still use it on the next round, with no penalty).

During the game we will also draw a lot of tiles with planets and technology on it, which happens to be very important for the gameplay. We will try to explore the planets, and study the technology. Those will give us very important victory points in the end, and will also have impact on our gameplay, because they have different mechanics, and will help us in different ways, so it's very important to create a somewhat reliable "engine".
 Because we're drawing tiles randomly, we will need some luck to get what we want. It's pretty easy to screw everything up, if we're not paying attention on what we're doing. Luckily there is always a way to replace unfinished tiles to other, random ones, even few of them at once.

I'm not making problems, if someone want to play it with me, but i can only play  this game max one, maybe two times, depends on the mood.  After that, game can be quite boring, because there's almost no interaction between players. Everyone is playing  their own game, making their own goals, with bigger one at the end of the game, which is counting victory points. Mechanic itself is good, but game's lacking some interaction, mostly negative one between players. Game is pretty popular, so maybe one day we will receive an expansion that will fix this problem. But if you love cold math, with predicting what your opponents will do, and you don't really like waiting for other players turn, you might like this game.
 And when you done playing, then you can use those nice looking cups, to drink something colorful. Like those cups.

RATING: 9.9
This game quickly entered my top 10. As expected, it provides a similar feel to Race for the Galaxy, but the dice mechanics smooth out the card flow economy experience. The game has a variety of mechanics to help mitigate luck (e.g. "dictate" and "reassign" powers), so getting the dice to do what you need is more strategy than luck. An immense amount of replay value is derived from the variety of starting combinations of Home World and Faction tiles. In addition, the number of tiles available in the bag is deep enough that you are unlikely to see the same combination of tiles twice. Another fantastic point to Roll is that the reduction in iconography (or rather, the text description of all abilities) makes the game a bit easier to teach than Race.

Amazing game - Race wins for me just for speed of play and comfort, but both are fantastic! Roll wins for new players though.

Another controversial point - lack of downtime outside of the Explore phase! It makes the game super quick, but can make it feel like solitaire.

RATING: 5
While I've played Race quite a bit and have respect for it, it's never been a personal favorite because I don't particularly care for games where the depth comes from high amounts of information to absorb rather than the intricacies of player interaction. Not that Race is entirely multiplayer solitaire, or even Roll for that matter, but these games are definitely too self-absorbed for my taste (I've come to be wary of any game with a personal tableau).

I give credit to Roll for being a clever dice implementation of the card game, but in practice it felt like more of the same and very monotonous. In sum, geeking out on dice roll odds (or card draw odds) isn't really my thing- I much prefer my opponents' decisions to be the main challenge I'm grappling with.

I just have to wonder if, once the novelty of playing Race for the Galaxy with dice wears off, I'll choose to play this over Race for the Galaxy.

RATING: 7
Roll for the Galaxy replicates Race for the Galaxy almost exactly, except it uses dice instead of cards. Despite the randomness inherent in die rolling, it does seem like Roll is "easier" due to the amount of luck mitigation built into the system, something that is not found in Race for the Galaxies card mechanics.

I have heard some people say that you can make a value judgement on the two games based on your preference of difficulty level. I'm not so sure, because this game is quite fun, regardless of the existence of Race for the Galaxy. Personally, I think there is room for both.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I very much enjoy this game, it has a great dice allocation mechanism and it's different enough from Race for the Galaxy to justify its own existence. But I have to say that it doesn't offer as many interesting decisions and "meat" (I guess it's because Roll for the Galaxy is more focused on dice management and luck mitigation rather than combo building - and I prefer the latter).  

The game is streamlined  in some areas, but contains some new aspects that initially might be just as confusing as the infamous "Race iconography" so I wouldn't neccessarily call Roll that much more "user friendly". Also, Race plays quicker, smoother and eats less table space.  

I watched and read many reviews saying that Roll completely replaces Race, but it doesn't (at least not for me). If you want a nicer looking, more inviting game then yes, go with Roll. But if all you care for is the rich, deep gameplay experience definitely stick to Race - it has less luck and gives you much more control. There's time and place for both games, but I would pick Race over Roll every time.

RATING: 7
If you like Race for the Galaxy, but think it's a bit too predictable with your hand of (random) cards, then you should try out Roll for the Galaxy. The hidden information in this game are the dice (instead of the cards) and those dice depict the available options you have when deciding what action to do: explore, develop, settle, produce, or ship. Happily, consume/trade is now just ship and you can either trade goods for money or earn points [b]and[/b] it happens after produce. I like how the tiles (cards?) that you're developing and settling are gained in the explore phase and that you're placing dice on those cards over the course of multiple turns (or in one if you can manage it).

This game is on my Want to Buy list and I'll probably add it to my Christmas wishlist this year.

RATING: 8
I have played this game twice now, and I am still not sure I understand the strategy of the game, but it is a lot of fun. Players roll dice simultaneously and place them on the types of actions they would like to take. Actions can provide income or help to expand a players empire with new worlds or technologies. Each world or technology provides new types of dice or different end scoring conditions for the player possessing them. The downside is that with all of the iconography and options it can be difficult to explain. This is a good game for gamers who can relate the rules to other mechanisms, but I don't think I would try to teach it to my family.

RATING: N/A
This is more than just a dice version of the classic that we played for so many hours. Although the crap ton of colored dice gave me Quarrior flashbacks.  It is still mostly a fun multi-player solitaire that introduces some very interesting game mechanics. We have ours at our side for reference. We aren't that competitive to spy on how the other players are placing their dice actions...mostly we don't spy. This will replace my card version of the game. I like the simultaneous play works for five players and still get in a whole game under 90 minutes.

RATING: 8.5
Wow, what a brilliant, brilliant dice-rolling game. The base game has 111 dice -- ONE HUNDRED ELEVEN! 

Each color dice is 'weighted' and is more likely to roll Explore, or Shipping, or Deleopment, etc. so depending on what you're most trying to accomplish will impact what dice you purchase for the next round.

Unfortunately, the theme isn't as appealing to my bride as some others, so it isn't hitting the table as often as I'd like. Fortunately, I do have some other gaming friends who are always up for a game of Roll ftG.

Update: My oldest suddenly got into Star Wars, and now has a hankering to play Roll ftG again! Woo-Hoo!

RATING: 9.6
Definitely my favorite dice game.

So many ways to mitigate luck and so satisfying to build our your empire or go nuts with a produce/ship engine.

Personal favorite player count is 3, but 4-5 still work they're just not as intense because practically every action is always selected. 

It's a bit easier to teach than Race, and arguably more fun for someone not after the dual hand managing craziness that is Race.

If I had one gripe it would be the noise of the cups + dice.

RATING: 8
I wasn't all that interested in Roll as Race didn't have much staying power for me... but I walked right to the counter and bought my own copy after getting a chance to play on a friend's copy.  Its unique these days for a dice game not to use the Yatzee mechanic and I really like how dice allocation works in Roll.  Also the text on the tiles minimalizes the barrier to entry for new players compared to Race which is nice.  In my first game the multi-player solitaire feel was very strong, but I'm hoping with more plays and particularly if I play with less than 5 people I will be able to keep track of other player's strategies more easily.  

I'm quickly coming to appreciate this as an accessible tableau building game someplace between a filler and a lighter Euro.  Should get a lot more play unless the "shiny" wears off too fast and I start being annoyed by the strong multi-player solitaire vibe.

RATING: 7
(Note, I'm a huge Race or the Galaxy fan, having played it many hundreds of times. This obviously impacts my thinking on Roll for the Galaxy)

After 10+ Plays: I've enjoyed playing this, but it hasn't grabbed me the way Race does.  I think what's holding it back a bit is the flow of resources.  In Race the cardflow seems pretty intuitive in its operation, and thus you can focus on building an engine.  In Roll, the diceflow is a bit clunky, and I think too much effort is expended just making sure you don't paint yourself into a corner where you end the turn with almost no dice in the cup.  I'll probably continue to play it, but it's never going to be in the same league as its cousin.

RATING: 9
I'm gonna be honest, the many custom dice play a big role in this rating. I do really love games that have you roll dice then have you do something with them (see also: Alien Frontiers). There are a lot of games that have you do something, then roll to resolve, which is often really unsatisfying. But using a random element to force you to be flexible is really cool, and there are tons of ways to mitigate that random element. That change alone puts it over Race, though I haven't played enough Race to get super good at it.

RATING: 8
Didn't get on with Race for the Galaxy at all, but I found this much more enjoyable - in every way. There's an amount of trying to second guess what your opponents are going to do so you can best capitalise on that action, but limited interaction beyond that.  However, the remainder of the game is quick, interesting and allows for a few different strategies to gain points (as well as the 'do I push to end the game or do I wait another turn or two to try to ensure I'm in the lead'). It is possible to cheat as dice are rolled behind your screen, but what can be done about that - just don't cheat, douche.

I'm very intrigued to see what expansions might bring - some hidden objectives and some more interaction would catapult this even further.
